RATING: 8
An interesting mix of tried and true Euro-game ideas misleadingly packaged as an Ameritrash game (it's not, it's definitely a Euro).

There are 8 rounds of repeating placement actions (think Caylus here), including such things as: turn order, boat-loading (for area-control purposes), merchant ships (for income generation), trade goods (for income and points), building purchasing (for special abilities, points, possibly income), discoveries (for income and points), specialists (for increased placements, think Leonardo da Vinci here), and war (used for area control).

The game is rather lengthy, but turns go quickly.  Seems to scale well (2-6), although I have only tried 2-5, and likely will not play this with 6.  There "seems" to be multiple paths to victory, and there's definitely turn angst nearly the whole game.  Does seem somewhat rough around the edges, but still it's a game that I think about when not playing, which is a rare but good sign for me.  Rating could easily rise.

Own the [boardgame=41261]Builders[/boardgame] expansion, including the [boardgame=114986]bonus tiles[/boardgame].  The expansion adds a few interesting elements to the game: 1) special starting abilities for each of the nations, this is a welcome addition and allows for some asymmetrical strategies, 2) more building tiles, which adds to the variety of the gameplay, and 3) an additional type of "worker", the builder- the builder has 2 abilities- makes buildings cheaper (powerful) and increases the points of controlled regions (powerful).  An easy expansion to integrate into the base game and a must have for fans of Age of Empires III.

Own all upgrades for [boardgame=173442]Glenn Drover's Empires: Age of Discovery - Deluxe edition[/boardgame].
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.9
A pretty solid game system that is nominally about exploration. It's really an 8 turn puzzle on trying to assemble the most victory points. The components are "ok" (bad VP track design) but I think the actual map territories should have been larger to accommodate pieces and create more clearly defined territories. If I had one overall criticism of the game, it would be that given the game is about 90% skill, why include the hidden discovery tile/card mechanism (which if a player fails, is incredibly punishing)?  Even though the discovery portion is a small part of the overall choices, it can have a large effect on the outcome of the game as you not only fail at achieving the Discovery VP's and money but you waste all of the men that could have been used to procure other things (magnified as it's an 8 turn game). It's a weird mechanism that is very American in a mainly Euro design. And some buildings are definitely more useful than others especially in Age III. 

Having said all that, the rest of the game works and creates some tricky decisions and gaming angst (if you're into that sort of thing). I'm not a Puerto Rico fan so keep that in mind.

RATING: 9.4
Juegazo eurogame, que funciona muy muy bien tanto con jugadores noveles como jugones. Colocación de trabajadores, parte de juego de mayorías, y diferentes modos de conseguir puntos, ambientado en la conquista y colonización de América. No tiene ninguna relación con el juego de PC. Me hice con él en inglés nuevo x 27€ ganga para todo lo que trae. Muy buen juego, excelente de mecánicas y componentes (excepto las losetas de edificio, que el grafismo podría ser mucho más claro). Escala perfecto de 3-5 jugadores, incluso se podría con 6 con una expansión que habría que ver si vale la pena lo que aporta (6o jugador, poderes asimétricos para todos, un nuevo especialista). Uno de mis euros favoritos con diferencia

RATING: 7
I played this game just once, so this are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Fun worker placement game with amazing - but still kinda unnecessary - components. I played this game just once, so this rating may go up or down, and I was really enjoying it. I liked the mechanics and the ideas behind the game. Yet during the scoring I realised the thing that made the rating go down for me: the buildings.

I LOVE games where you can build something, but here this part feels too random and also unbalanced. There're building in the III Era that are simply huge amounts of points. I know it's up to the players to find a way to get the hands on them, but by the end I think this just diminishes the importance of the player skills. You can find yourself suddently a lot back on the point track just because someone placed a worker on the turn order before you two turns ahead and you couldn't do anything about it, because this kept happening turn after turn.

RATING: 3
While playing Age Of Empires III, it felt like a great game with lots of promise. The game itself is easy to pick-up once you start playing, and it seemed to flow nicely.

My problem with AOE3 is scoring and how difficult it was to determine who was in the lead in terms of position, which made it challenging to figure out who to target during warfare. I dislike games that don't have a somewhat accurate representation of how a player is doing during game play that involves anything to do with attacking another player.

In my 5 player game, the distribution was something like 20 points at the end of age 2. At the end of age 3, the scoring for the round doubled or more for all players. After the scoring phases, the total scores were between 69-72. I was fairly disappointed at how close the game scored - we all had somewhat different strategies, but it seemed like it didn't really make a big difference in the end  - we all had nearly the same number of points.

Argh, I'm so sleepy right now, can't articulate what I liked and didn't like about AOE3, and I don't have any energy to extensively complain about the board game layout - except to say that for a map game that includes placement of many game pieces, AOE3 is wildly unaccommodating. 

*** UPDATE 1: After reviewing my ratings, I've decided to lower this one. I would never pick to play this game - so many other great games to choose from! ***

*** UPDATE 2: Lowered this rating again, since the whole package is unattractive and the pieces are hard to distinguish. If this had a better board game design, I would rank it higher. It's now in the trade pile for grooven. ***

BGG review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/172207

Grooven traded this one for 1960: Making of the President at a Tabscon math trade in Dec 2009.

RATING: 9
My exceedingly few negatives about this game are entirely component related: there should be coins worth five (instead of only ones and tens), and it can, at times, be difficult to distinguish between the different types of colonists.
60% Euro, 40% Ameritrash; almost like a kind of Risk for adult eurogamers! The preponderance of the Euro will keep the conflict and suppression-of-aboriginals elements from being a turn off for most people, IMO. Those features are abstracted just enough to keep from being a problem. 
I'm quite surprised at how easy it is to catch on to the rules. The simplicity of the Euro with the chrome and theme of Ameritrash, I guess. A wonderful degree of tension every turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
2009-12-17
A fun worker placement game where you have to judge your opponents placement to try to get the best spots while still getting everything else you want in that round.  It plays very similarly to Caylus or Agricola in this aspect, but there is also an exploratory and combat element to the game.  It would be very difficult to win using a military strategy, but it adds an element to the game that I feel is lacking from the other worker placement games.

RATING: 9
Even though I don't play it as often as I'd like AoE III is always a fantastic experience. It scales well 2-5 players, though is very different for each. It's a Euro, but with the sense of an evolving story; just enough randomness, lots of different strategies to explore, and lots of tough tactical decisions as the slots for worker placement fill up. I love it, and really want to play more often!

My criticisms are to do with the components: a useless scoring track, the necessity of printing some BGG play aids (easily done, that one), and the difficulty of telling the (rather garishly coloured) pieces apart. The game is good enough to overcome these problems, but a new edition where they were fixed would be truly outstanding.

...

Years on, although I still play fairly infrequently I still love it, really enjoy it every time I play.

RATING: 7.5
A game with very little new.  The bits it has taken from other games - Puerto Rico, El Grande, Keydom, etc, are moulded together reasonably well.  However the two stage training of pieces felt negative and I am not a great fan of direct conflict games, which this is in part.  A couple of the tiles seemed very strong, especially the first age tile giving 20 coinage, immediately allowing the acquisition of two further tiles.  The board was needlessly large.  A standard A2 board would have been better, saving shelf space.  However, a step up from most of its predecessor Eagle Games, making Tropical Games one to watch.

Notwithstanding the foregoing, a second game was much more enjoyable and to my surprise I find myself increasing my rating.  I now hope for more plays to confirm my view.

RATING: 5
Read the rules and very pleased by it.  Seems to be a very solid Euro game with a heavy feel to it.  The fact that it should be able to handle 6 players, is a huge plus.  Ready to purchase.

First Game: 6-15-07
Finally played the game and it is a lot of fun.  There is a lot of things going on.  It is pretty cool as it kind of carries you along a strategy due to buildings that you get from the beginning of the game.  However, this game also rewards plays from the next level that takes into account what others are doing.  The game is pretty tight as you only have so many colonists to deploy.  You cannot do everything you want to do.  I am very happy to have this in my collection as it is a solid game and that it can handle 6 players.  It is hard to find games of this strategic weight and handle 6 players at the same time.  The only one I can think of is Age of Steam with the Netherlands map and Power Grid somewhat.
Initial Rating: 8

UPDATE: 9-1-07
This is the 2nd game that I played using the Official Variant (all discovery tiles are known) and I enjoy the game a lot more.  With it, the game is definitely a lot more deep and strategic.  A new layer of strategy needs to be taken into consideration.  Also, with the official variant, another way is available to gather doubloons to purchase capital buildings.  Before if you just luck out of trade goods, you are in a bad shop as every turn passes you get left behind in the capital buildings race.  With the Official Variant the game is now solidly an 8.  Now I just gotta find a way to fix the Age III buildings.
Rating 8 -> 8

Update: 17-Oct-07
I just played a game that was horrible.  Maybe I am still emotional about it, but it wasn't fun for me at all.  It was mainly due to an opponent that decided to kill my colonist instead of another player which had much more potential (i.e. more income and buildings) than me and I was dead last.  This certainly put me on tilt.  I declared that my colonist must be avenged no matter what and that I shall see blue men scattered about the land.  Then another player said that if I do that it will break the game.  I said I didn't care, but I lied.  So I tried to be rationale about it and just try to come back after after I was just kicked while I was down.   Sigh...  I wrote a lot more to put my thoughts on paper but I accidentally clicked on something which made me just lose a lot of what I already wrote down.  I am not going to rewrite them.  Bottom line is that once you are down in this game it is very hard to come back, unless you get left alone and try to recover.  Unfortunately, you can't do that because you'll just get hit because you are weak and an easy target.  This game really encourages events that kicks you while you are down.  If I do not already own this game, I would never suggest to play this.  But since I own it, I'll give it another chance and maybe come up with another variant for the game.  I just find so many things wrong with this game.
Update: 8 -> 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Played twice.  I like that you can acquire various types of "specialists" that have certain advantages.  I also like the area control, the impact that warfare had on the areas to control, and discovering even more areas to control.  Based on actual gameplay, I'd say that AoE is a 2nd or 3rd tier worker placement game, but it does offer something different than Caylus, Agricola or Leonardo da Vinci.   

AoE has a random order in which the buildings appear.  Some buildings are clearly better than others and if you get to go first that round, then you clearly get the best building.  However, you could also select to go first for the next round and randomly get a bunch of mediocre buildings to choose from.  Do I even have to mention how random Discovery is?  Overall AoE III is way too random for the length of time it takes to play.

Gameplay: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:

RATING: 7
The old world exploits the new world. Gotta be the most hackneyed theme in Eurodom. Add in the play of indentured servitude and hordes of dead natives that conveniently disappear and you've pretty much got a Eurogame lamb in Ameritrash wolf's clothing.

I absolutely HATE the use of primary colors for the colonist/specialist figures. It's a shame because all of the other components are quite nice. The game itself is pretty fun and astoundingly straightforward. Long term planning can be accomplished with experience of knowing which buildings can come out in stage II and III. Short term planning, bluffing/psychology abounds. No puzzle here, just figuring out what other people will want to do and doing it before them. A few different types of strategy to try still. But you probably can't win without some good income in the mid game.

Confrontation can be overt or subtle. You can lose as many colonists to your opponent in the merchant ship competition as you can in battles or warfare. So use this to your advantage when playing with vengeful/victim types.

It's refreshing to see a game play true to it's duration statement. Easily done in two hours, if you don't have to teach. Still, game is so straightforward that it's pretty easy to teach too.

RATING: 8
Very interesting and nicely balanced. Much forced interaction and competition and a lot of 'grab this before he does', generally very enjoyable, lots of paths to victory and it seems like the winner is not clear until the final round, a solid 8. Minor gripes, the pieces are nice (though sometimes it is hard to distinguish the merchant from the colonists) but the box is poor quality and the board artwork isn't very ergonomic - score track is very difficult to follow (esp at corners), areas are often long and thin meaning it is difficult to keep track of which pieces are where or which territories join which others towards the end. Strangely, also no marked spot for the trade goods or capital buildings.

After another couple of plays it is clear that the way to win this one is, like many other games, to watch which route the others take and to choose a route without competition. Buying a building pretty much every turn also seems to be key.

In these respects I found it similar to PR, though the wider variety of options and the greater interaction in the colonies made it more fun in my opinion.

RATING: 7
3 plays, Fun game of economics and exploration.  Blends euro-game mechanics and a light (area control) wargame.  Multiple paths to victory.  Good balance of luck, tactics & strategy.  Some buildings are a bit too powerful and slightly unbalanced, but I hear that there is a more balanced set posted here on BGG.  Moderate learning curve since you really need knowledge of the building tiles to do well.  Probably best with 3-4p and too chaotic with 5-6p.  Nice tension over discovery/exploration since conquering a map region puts the trade goods token up for grabs on the next turn... so hopefully you planned ahead and have a missionary to snatch it up.

** UPDATE 2010: after play 3, it feels like this game takes a bit too long to play, but it's probably just my slow turns and the fact that all of my games have included at least 1 newbie.

** UPDATE 2014: I don't like the luck of the colony points or the range (4-7 pts); it should be tighter or balanced with money (i.e., a low points colony shouldn't also have low money).  The game encourages you to conquer regions by attacking with the strength of 6 guys to avoid losses, but then it is a crap shoot whether you get as many points as your opponent who is attacking a different region.

** UPDATE 2015: I need to try AoEIII with the rule tweaks from the new/deluxe GD's Age of Discovery... (new rule #1) reduce the risks associated with discovery, and (new rule #2) reveal the Age 3 buildings at the start.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Fine civ-style worker placement game. Of course its all about the VPs but there are a variety of ways of accumulating them: discovering new unexplored regions, controlling colonies, accumulating trade goods (in sets), acquiring buildings which can yield extra VPs while giving you other (at times quite powerful) in-game benefits.

Your basic workers are replenished at the start of each round (there are eight altogether divided into three epochs), but there are means to get specialized actors (Captains, soldiers, merchants, & missionaries) which can be over and above your basic workers and provide specialized benefits at the various tasks one undertakes. A round consists of, in turn, placing of all your actors then the activities are resolved and finally income is generated (which can be money from trade goods and/or additional specialized actors). At the end of each epoch, a scoring phase takes place where points for the control of colonies is awarded. At the end of epoch III (and the game) all the additional points for discoveries, income generation capacity, and points awarded by special buildings are all added to one's final totals.

The game flows smoothly and provides an endless series of key decision points. As in any well designed worker-placement game, your need to do more than you can manage provides continuous tension. After just one play I am drawn to this game and wonder at how it evaded me for so long (perhaps too long as it is long OOP). We found three players worked well and even though a new player (me) had to be taught the game, it came in under three hours. Highly recommended.

RATING: 10
Designers shouldn't rate their own games.  I just couldn't help it.  I really like this game. :)
The theme is one of my favorites and permeates the game, making the mechanics very intuitive. The gameplay offers the players many deliciously hard decisions that seem small when taken individually, but add up to success or failure in the long run. The game rewards long-term planning, but rewards flexibility and adaptation to opponents' choices even more. There are many long-term strategies and combinations that can be tried (My favorites are: The Missionary/ Colonizer Strategy; The Captain/ Merchant/ Privateer Strategy; The Conquistador/ Discovery Strategy; The Merchant/ Trader Strategy).  Some may work in one situation and not in others.  All this adds up to a game that is extremely re-playable and fun.  

Wooden cubes or meeples can always be substituted by the players who prefer them, but miniatures actually work best in this design!

RATING: 8
This was an instant hit with both my sons and myself. Other friends playing felt the same level of excitement playing this boardgame version of the video game. The conflict builds quickly as players compete for the placement of their colonists in the event boxes to get a foothold over their opponents for discoveries and accumulating trade goods for points. Other placement features must be considered to accomplish these goals, such as gaining a merchant vessel, or extra buildings, to name a few. A fun and challenging eight turns of tactical decisions and achievement. I'm not sure the buildings are so unbalanced, though others seem to think this is the case. I have seen a clear victory accomplished by amassing trade goods without purchasing many buildings. Additionally, players could position themselves to purchase or even block, so the more "powerful" buildings are not acquired by another player. This is a true gamer's game, so be prepared!

RATING: 6
It plays like a euro, but without any of the checks and balances of a euro. The buildings can fundamentally unbalance games, leading to runaway winners. In our game, the winner beat second place by 30% and last place by 100%!! 

Why play a Wallace-wannabe when you can simply reach for the real McCoy if you want this type of game?

A lot of strategy can go into territory control, set collecting of trade goods, getting ships (=wild cards) and recruiting specialists BUT that can all be defeated by being at the right place in line when the right building comes up for purchase. 

Puerto Rico has buildings of different powers and values but it is balanced. All buildings are available to all players at all times, limited only by availability. PR has a shipping strategy that balances these buildings. AoE III lacks this check. The session reports on BGG tell of a lot of lobsided wins in AoE III and my experience confirms that.

The one positive opinion I have of AoE III is that it does a good job of relating to its theme. The components, figures of the specialists, moving of colonists of Europe from the right side of the board to the Americas all add to the feel of recreating New World colonization by European powers. This game has the feel of a euro but with Ameritrash killing & conflict, all based on the Caylus/Pillars of the Earth platform.

But in the end, it could not fix this game.

Things to remember - give extra coins in the initial set up for second, third, etc..; when you send a merchant to the new world you get 5 coins; specialists count in the three required to take the trade good in the New World and in the scoring; initiative is resolved just before the beginning of the next turn.

RATING: 6
Solid worker-placement game with lots of tension and a great sense of progression over the course of the game. The buildings and specialists direct which route you will focus on for a win, and make for some interesting gameplay decisions. 

Unfortunately, the game is terrible with two-players, and should not be listed as a 2-p. game without considerable changes to the rules and/or board. As written, an early building advantage can cause a terrible runaway leader problem in the two-player game, especially when using the "instant effect" buildings and national advantage tiles from the Builder expansion. Also, it doesn't make sense to limit the Colonist space but not the other spaces, making it possible in a 2-p. game for someone to get 20-25 goods tokens in a single game.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Well, what to say on this one. I was biased against it coming in, since Drover's games have been so universally awful and because I sort of resent him clogging the market with dreck. I thought we might be rid of him when Eagle collasped so spectacularly; but no.

Still, in honesty, Age of Empires III is really not that bad. It works. But what it is, once again, is a game that captures the worst of both worlds: the abstract themelessness of a German game combined with the gratuitous randomness of an American game. The impact of luck on this game is gargantuan: the expedition results span a huge range from total screwage to huge bonanzas. And the order the buildings come out in is a major random impact. I'm not fundamentally opposed to big randomness in general, but if you're going to go that way, I prefer the game be a) short, and b) not so bloody pretentious. So the new Thebes works for me, despite big randomness, because it's short, well-themed, and doesn't try to be more than it is. AoEIII, on the other hand, is extremely random while being long, weakly-themed, and trying to be everything to everyone. Maybe it's not a euro/amero divide after all, maybe it's just that those Germans just plain have more experience and are better at making boardgames.

Just to be clear here, too, the theme on Age of Empires III is a total paste-up. Virtually everything is totally abastract. Your average well-done German game, whether it be Settlers: Struggle for Rome, Puerto Rico, Lord of the Rings, Mystery at the Abby, whatever, has a lot better theme. American games need to stop trying to play the theme card, which often consists of no more than nice plastic figures, and start getting working games. Fantasy Flight may have a wide range of issues, but they at least seem to understand how to do theme, generally, if not much else; Drover is trying to emulate the Germans without the talent to do it himself or the support structure to cover for his weaknesses (just to be clear, I don't think he is without talent; if he understood that he needs to build an organization rather than crank out his own half-baked designs, he might be able to do some good stuff).

Oh yeah, one more thing: the physical design on this game is completely atrocious.

All in all, Age of Empires III may have flashy bits, but for the discriminating gamer, there is extremely little on offer here that hasn't been done vastly better by many, many other games.

Sorry, one last thing, then I'll stop ranting I promise: this is what Brian Bankler would call a "fixed fun" game. There is only so much fun available in this game, and adding more players just ensures that each player gets less fun. So fewer players is probably better (3-4). But on the other hand, the game seems clearly targetted for 5, and the effort to scale for fewer seems weak.

RATING: 9
Having a lot of plays of this under my belt now, all I can say is that the goddamn thing just works. There are lots of strategic options that are balanced, but not in a way that makes you feel like every action is arbitrary because it scores the same amount of points. The end of the game creates a lot of tension as the new world gets crowded. 

I think the theme is well woven into the mechanics, and have no problem with the expeditions. I have rarely seen luck play an overwhelming part in the winner. But sending less than the max for expeditions is a fun gamble and I really like that element of the game. 

It is a game that requires knowledge of all the buildings to be played well, so playing with various experience levels will not work. With a dedicated group though, this shines. 

I will say that I think it does not handle 5 (or more) very well. Since there are a static number of points to be made in the New World and from goods, the points available per player declines as player count increases. So Expeditions become very powerful, and with it the buildings that support expeditions. This creates turn order problems, and despite adding players makes the game less interactive and vicious.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
AoE is a very cool worker placement game with area majority scoring rules. It plays over 8 rounds spanning 3 ages in which you score each age (and then you score a lot of bonus points at the end of the game). In a sense it’s a much simpler version of Dominant Species as you place your workers on a track, and when every player has placed his workers, they activate from top to bottom.

I like how you have 5 different workers at your disposal, and that these have various powers which give you extra income/units on the board/help you win battles etc. The fact that you have different types of workers really enhances my enjoyment of the game! You need to get new workers, send them to the new world to get goods and score you points, get merchant ships, fight battles against the other players, go on expeditions in undiscovered areas and fight the native, collect trading goods and build new buildings – so you really need to do a lot in the game, but the overall gameplay isn’t too hard to understand. The strategies are plentiful, though! 

A couple of things I dislike are these: the exploration is a huge bummer to me as it’s so random – you can send 4 units and lose, while another player can send 1 soldier and still win… That’s really a bad game design I think, and in my games I’ve removed the 1 and 5 tiles so that the luck is less profound. But it still sucks so much when you send 3 guys on an expedition and you fail, because the penalty is quite severe. This means that most of us just wait until we have a strength of 4 until we go on expeditions. The other spot on the board where everybody can place units is the merchant ship, and I also hate that only the winner gets the ship, and the losers lose every single units they spent… Which makes it very important to win (and in general the merchant ships are very handy in order to get income during the game, so they are important). Some buildings are better than others, and it can be a little frustrating that you don’t know which endgame bonus buildings show up in age 3 as they can have a big impact on your whole strategy.

Having read the rules for the expansion, I cannot wait to try that! It sounds as if it makes a great game even better. AoE works with all player counts, but I have to say that, as is the case with most area majority games, it works better with more players. With few players there are also too many open spots on the board to place your workers on. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Totally love this game on one play, so it can only go down but I hope it doesnt.  It has elements of Leanadro Da Vinci, Puerto Rico and El Grande, but with some interesting and fun ways these elements mix with each other.  You go around the table placing workers in areas that represent actions, and for most of them you want to be there first to get the best benefits.  One of the actions is sending people to the New World, where the game suddenly feels like El Grande and you are trying to get majority or second majority to score points three times during the game.  But you cant move guys into these new world regions until they are opened up by discovering them (another action) which is another way to score points.  Lastly, you are also trying to build up an income infrastructure so that you can build buildings with special powers such as extra workers or big end game point getters.  Its an exciting blend of three games but with extra elements like uprading your workers to have special abilities for the next turn. This one plays with little downtime and quick decisions, yet it offers a wonderful strategical backbone for scoring points combined with the tactical placement of workers.  Add in too many things you want to do on your turn and this one shines with the brightest of them. Oh, and the bits are amazing.

RATING: 7
Age of Empires III (or now Glen Drover's Empires) is worker placement and area control done right. It's one of the early flock of worker placement games, but in my estimation, it has held up very well. 

One of the reasons I like it is because the game feels different from the typical "Euro cube pushers." It looks gaudy, with an awkward map and tons of plastic pieces. But behind the plastic and gaudiness is a solid design, with plenty of angst in choosing your locations for your workers, and a lot of jockeying with other players. I haven't played it a ton, but I've seen multiple viable paths to victory. 

Some of the pieces are easy to confuse, so I ended up painting the bases different colors (white, gray, gold, brown) to better distinguish the "special" units.

Update -- ultimately sold this one off. It's not a bad game... it just never got played in my local group. I didn't enjoy it enough to try and lobby to get it to the table.

RATING: 3
Tentative rating.

Summary plays:

0) (prototype)  Unanimous summary: Please stay with the wood cubes and small(er) board.  (Instead production version went with annoying plastic and a bigger board)

  1) 6 players is too many.  4 is fairly clearly the sweet spot.  Toss the plastic.  Use poker chips and wood cubes from other games.  

  2) Never bothered with the plastic, went straight to wood cubes and glass bits.  Game called on account of time and lack of interest.  Not clear that playtime is stable.

  2) 6 players is too many, go for 4.  Lose the plastic and go with wood bits.  Can't see the board for plastic, they're unstable, and they just get in the way.   From a German game design vantage this has the core of an extremely good game wrapped centred on the inefficient translation of multiple currencies, but it has been run away from and hidden with extraneous and distracting crap.  The central good game has now been fairly thoroughly lost.  Too much chrome, too much variance in goods and buildings, contention is too lumpy, not enough value for planning, too much of a hormonal boo-yah feel-good game.  Doesn't seem to have isolated its audience.  It is strangely common to not know whose turn it is.  Needs a conductor.

Personal summary: Overly chaotic with too little reward for forward planning beyond risk management and positional flexibility.  More of an experience game than a competitive excellence game.  Nice concepts of multiple currencies but exchange rates and methods are poorly implemented and arguably unbalanced and disconnected in several areas and then the economy is regularly sabotaged by luck factors. A bit of a systems game, but without sufficient connection among the systems, thus players are forced to advance along several systems fronts in order to attempt risk management.  Remarkably poor presentation (plastic bits are overly large, unstable and and indistinguishable/undifferentiated, board is twice the size it would need to be with reasonable bits, art is super-saturated/garish with poor use of focus and visual direction).

RATING: 4
It's an Eagle...ahem...Tropical game that I would play again, so yes, this is vastly surpassing my expectations.

AoE is another in the increasingly vast stable of designs that uses the "place piece on this square to get thing, no one else can occupy the same square" mechanic, but then combines that with area influence, a bit of combat, some special power buildings, and a touch of exploration.

At the start of the game, each player has 5 standard workers. These are used just like Caylus workers, they are set onto spots to generate effects. Some can gain goods, which when collected in sets, create income. Others can be sent across to the discovered areas, while others can be sent to discover new places. There are also special people types such as missionaries (who create an extra piece when they arrive at a colony), merchants (who have various ways of generating money), captains (who are good for discovering new places), and soldiers (who can aid area majority fights by shooting people).

So if I had to nutshell this it would be Caylus, with more random elements, but also with a few more things going on. Random elements include the trade goods that come up (important because sets of at least 3 are much better than singletons/pairs), the order the buildings come up (also important if you want to pursue particular strategies), and probably most critically, the discovery tiles, where you gain points and income if you have enough to defeat the natives, which are randomly of strength between 2 and 6. The discovery thing reminded me strongly of War! AoI, a game I am not at all fond of. If you gamble (i.e.: take less than the full allotment) and get hammered by the natives, you have just lost many, many actions. When you couple this with the "ok but not amazing" payout structure for discovery, one can see why people complain about the randomness of those natives.

There are some things to like here though. Unlike Caylus, or a lot of the other titles coming out in this genre, there is significant differentiation in player positions based on the buildings they can acquire, which give special privileges ala Puerto Rico. (Of course, now that Agricola exists, differentiation of player positions is taken care of by another game as well). Anyway...almost all of the buildings seem very good in certain situations or combinations, although it does feel like the ones that generate bonus pieces into your pool are more powerful, because those will in turn give you more actions which, at worst will be useful for sending to the colonies (area majority fights) or to discover (some decent spare points). Not surprisingly, these are put out randomly, so even being high in the order gives one no guarantees.

Falling behind the piece curve seemed to be a sure-fire recipe for defeat in our game, and the winner had the largest stable of free guys each turn. I think this does highlight another thing though, the early game economy. Generating money is a real bear. It is used mainly for buying buildings, which can give that nasty differentiation I mentioned above) but the only real way to make money quickly is to either get one of the buildings that generates money (probably my first choice), or to get the building that spawns merchants. Having access to that money will in turn beget more money, more buildings, and a bigger number of actions each turn. Thinking about it some more, I just don't see how someone could win if they weren't keeping up with the "guy pace" set by the person with the most buildings.

Scaling is something I wonder a bit about as well. With 5, the competition for specialist guys and any of the finite demand areas becomes heated, and people end up sending guys out to discover just because there is nothing else to do with them. By contrast, it seems like 3 would be very loose. This is all because there is no built in scaling to the action part of the board. It seems like it would probably be best with 4 because of that. I would not want to play this with 6.

Playing this with 5 also highlighted another huge problem for this game, probably my biggest beef, and that's the size of the left side of the board coupled with the bits. Not big enough. The colonies are thematically depicted, for the most part, by long, skinny land masses. This is fine when only a couple of pieces are present, but when the pieces are taking over, it becomes very difficult to tell who is where. This is just bad graphic design. The pieces, while neat in theory, also can be very hard to tell apart at a distance (or when they're lined up tightly in the colonies) and I think they exacerbate the graphic design problems. If I really liked this game that much, and owned it, I would probably do a graphic re-design of the map with big, square colonial areas, and would likely also replace the pieces with wood blocks or some sort of clear, distinct shapes to make them easier to tell apart at a glance. Being able to tell what types of pieces your opponent has should not require asking them specifically (and perhaps giving away your plans in the process).

All of this aside, I think the game itself is decent, in a relative sense. I don't think there is much in the way of new ground here, but the melding of stuff comes together and at least works. I do think there is a not insignificant degree of luck in certain phases of the game, but for the designer, this is expected. The game feels like it has a lot of rough edges though, and the balance of the strategies doesn't feel like it's there. Early advantages seem to snowball, and it can be difficult to offensively interfere with those leaders because they have more resources than you do. Though I initially rated it a 5, I would tend to avoid playing this in most situations, so it has to be dinged back to a 4. At least it's better than Pillars of the Earth!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5

RATING: 5
A simple worker placement game without too much fuss, although it suffers from undue chrome and subsequent usability issues. The basic game is not too bad, but contains nothing worth writing home about. Workers can be used to place them in provinces (for VP and a little trade), or used in an effort to secure trade goods (for money and at the end some VP) or used to gain more workers, ... And so forth. Repeat for 8 rounds, note who obtained the highest VP score, done.

What I didn’t particularly like about this game is that the discovery tiles and cards are pretty much a crap shoot, and that buildings necessary for some strategy paths may or may not turn up. In the first game I played I found myself investing heavily in good tiles, which at round 5 or 6 caused me to conclude I wouldn’t score highly. You don’t get many points for them at the end on their own, and it leaves you vulnerable to New World tactics of other players. The approach must be more of a mixed bag. Okay, I can dig that. But now for the clinger: My partner happened to invest heavily in goods too, had even more of these than I dud, and happened to be first when the third age started... With a beautiful tile ‘1 VP per good tile’ up for sale. Stating that was like trusting the cat to keep the cream would be an understatement. She won by a margin of about 20 points. So there you have the nasty catch: since you can’t claim both first place in turn order as well as first place somewhere else you‘re forced to gamble. It may work out; it may also not work out. I’m not amused that this can make or break a player’s chances at winning the game. Even with a more mixed strategical approach for all of us there would still be the uncertainty of buildings benefiting one player significantly more than can be made up by the other players through other means. 

Something similar applies to the discoveries. I can build up a significant force only to find out it wasn't at all necessary, and that my investment was pretty much wasted. It can also happen the other way around, when a force isn't sufficient, and then quietly curse the additional 'bonus' of having told the rest of the world what they need in order to secure that particular tile. In summary: the game actively hinders you in performing an action by making the outcome of that action you've committed to uncertain. It's not as bad as in other titles, but the beginnings of dislike are there just the same.

The result is that I'm wondering whether it'll be worth investing in further. I think it's kinda cute that most negative comments are by now so old they only mention the hot games of those days (Caylus, Pillars Of The Earth, even Leonardo da Vinci), so let me add one more which will cause some to wonder WTF? in a few years' time: Given the subject matter of colonisation and discovery, I would really prefer Endeavor over this not too fragrant offering. Endeavor is gamewise a far more challenging and rewarding title, scales a lot better too, emphasises skill over dumb luck (the game doesn't even have luck), and is ergonomically a lot more pleasing as well. Endeavor sees too little table time as it is already too.

In short, I don’t expect that the game’s rating will ever rise above 5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
One of the greatest joys of being a collector is when you
finally discover an old classic that hits on all levels.
It has everything on my check list for a good game.
Area Control / set collecting / worker placement / easy to explain rules / agonizing decisions / money management
/ colonization theme - check - check - check ......

This game just set the standard of how I now will measure
every other game I own. The only random variable in the game is when you flip the discovery tiles/cards. Yet it is a calculated risk with clear and precise values to base Your decisions on. Failure to discover should not get you upset , but you will feel like cursing your fate if unsuccessful.

Tips for first game: Copy all the building tiles for each age on seperate pieces of paper and make sure all players are aware of building abilities beforehand. Nobody likes to lose because they didn`t know there were some powerful buildings that come up in different ages.

Sadly this game is ( OOP )Out Of Print ,but maybe a reprint is in the making. I hope new generations will get a chance to play this excellent game.

RATING: 6
Fun game - interesting ebb and flow of the usefulness of various parts of the board over the course of play. Multiple paths to victory, though the colonies are a huge source of points; you really can't afford to ignore them entirely. Can take a long time if your playgroup is AP-prone.

I prefer two tweaks to the expedition rules: (1) only the player making an expedition looks at the tile/card (so even if you're annihilated, you've gained possibly-useful scouting information - if it was a card, you may keep it for re-draw next time if you'd like); and (2) when you switch to the exploration deck, players may use N exploration-colonists to draw N additional exploration cards (of which they then choose one to explore, using their remaining colonists)

*March 2013* Did I mention just how awesome this game is? Just played two more games, and it was great. I won both times, but the great part is that I used quite different stratgies for each game. The first was a trade/wealth approach, the second was a colonization/trade path. Any game that allows players to win using radically different strategies is a winner in my book!

RATING: 8

RATING: 5.5
Based on one 5-player game it plays long and is prone to AP. Worker placement with some limited development/engine building and some limited take that in pursuit of area majority VP's and bonus tile scoring. It's a solid design and if this is the type of game you like this will be right up your street. I've grown a bit weary of this kind of game so it's not really for me.

The theme is good but the game doesn't really engage with it in the sense of capturing the original PC game. The take that element of the game is relatively limited which means it's possible for a player to be on the recieveing end of more than his fair share of it, and If you fall behind early you probably stay behind.

Would play again but only with 4 or less players and I don't need to own it.

RATING: 8
I'm starting this out at an 8.  I'm not a big fan of area-influence games. RATING: 4
While it looks like Age of Empires III had the potential to be a good game at some point, I suspect that some of the attempts to make it lighter ultimately killed it as a game for me. The numerous individualized random elements added to the game make entire sections of it reduced to a gamble, making it so potentially winning through those areas is very hit and miss. And while you can avoid many of those elements by focusing on the area majority part of the game, you can't avoid the importance of the random civilization tiles (not all of which are guaranteed to come out) or the random trade good tiles without potentially killing yourself entirely. 

So in summary I consider it an interesting game, but it is one of the few were the random elements of the game really kill it for me. So it gets a big negative vote from me.

RATING: 7
Players play a country, colonising the Americas and discovering new lands.  Players take turns allocating colonists in Caylus fashion to specific roles for the upcoming turn.  Then the roles are resolved - colonies spring up, trade goods, merchant shipping, capital infrastructure, discover new lands, etc.  It's all in the quest for victory points and these are earned via region majorities at three points during the game, discoveries, trade income and infrastructure value.  Nice game, very balanced, with a sense of escalating tension as it moves towards a conclusion.  Lots of ideas borrowed from other games, but the theming, engrossing, interactive game play, and a sensible time limit cap earns this one a spot in the collection.  Components are mixed, ranging from great (miniatures, tiles) to very poor (cards, box).  Good game, though.  Rating bumped up a notch after trying the two player game.

RATING: 7.25

My rating is staying the same at this point, but I foresee it moving slightly down sometime in the future.

[b]6.20.10 Update:[/b] This game took a big hit today. The luck just keeps getting more and more annoying. I've dropped this more than a whole point and removed it from my top ten. Unfortunate.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I love the computer game of this name, but was pretty pissed when I got this boardgame and saw how it had no relation whatsoever to the computer game.  It's like the computer game maker completely sold out the name of his game just to make a little extra cash from the royalties.

My group actually liked this game the first few times we played it, but then discovered its lack of replay value really soured us on it.  Finally, there's the way the tiles you buy pretty much determine your strategy for the rest of the game.  For example, if you buy the tile that makes it easier or cheaper to do something, you're pretty much forced to concentrate on specializing in doing that thing for the rest of the game.  And once you've let the early game tiles steer your strategy that way, you're REALLY forced into buying whatever late game tiles reward you for your particular area of specialization.

Gee, whaddaya know.  The guy who bought the early-game tile that makes his ships more valuable also wants to buy the late-game tile that rewards him for having lots of ships.  Who-da thunk it?

We hated the injun-killin' tiles, too.  The line between victory and defeat was just too fine, too total.  Barely have enough strength to beat the indians, and the world is yours on a plate.  Barely fail, though, and you get absolutely nothing for your efforts, wasting a whole round of play for you.  And if you "overkill" the indians, beating them by a mile and a half, and you've simply wasted a lot of effort on them.  The only winning "strategy" for exploring the injun tiles was to barely have what you needed to beat them.  Anything extra was wasted, and falling short was total disaster.

After 3-4 games of this, I couldn't trade it fast enough.  I might get suckered into playing it again, but it certainly doesn't need to be cluttering up my collection of games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10

RATING: 7.75
I was pretty taken with this game when I first played it.  In a lot of ways, it's pretty derivative, just a bunch of area majority and set collection tacked on to a worker placement engine.  It really is more than the sum of its parts, though.  There are a number of areas for you to focus in, and you have to decide what you want your empire to do with its time & resources.  

There are certainly some rough edges that you have to get past.  The scoring track is terrible; just use paper.  
The special buildings vary wildly in power level, with many solid ones, some less useful ones, a few very narrow ones, and then a few stinkers which are pretty much never worth it.  (I've considered going through and buffing some of them, to see if they can be salvaged, but I don't get to play this often enough to do real testing.  The other option is to simply cut them.)  
Exploration takes so many people to do (if you want to be sure of success), and could reward you with nothing if you skimp out, that it feels a little weaker than the other possible actions.  
Trade goods can also be frustrating, if the ones you're trying to collect never come out or show up all at once.  
I forgive it these flaws, because I enjoy the game as a whole.

RATING: 8.5
One of the earliest worker placement games and still top drawer.  No dice, but a bit of luck, and always  a tight strategy game.  Very good and one of the few w/p games to have different workers (Manhattan Project/Energy Empire).  The box is a too big and flimsy, certain pieces hard to differentiate and no relation really to the video game (only name and theme perhaps), but overall a Euro game where the theme actually makes sense and works!  AoE III permits multiple paths to victory (unlike say Agricola), without a runaway leader problem.  Works well 2-5, though of course more conflict with higher player counts.  I appreciate the extra cards, building s and trade good counters meaning no game will ever play out exactly the same.

I haven't played the builders expansion featuring player powers yet, nor GMT's Dominant Species - a heavier version of this system.  Not sure what Tom Vassel is on about with saying ships being overpowered, but suspect they overlooked the 1 ship per trade set limit, so I don't see any flaw or 'nerf' requirement.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This one starts off at a 7 for me.  I don't really like this much direct conflict in a game.  I like that there is no dice rolling for combat, but the fact that you can essentially 'exicute' anybody who doesn't also have a soldier present is a little excessive.  

I don't like the all or nothing bidding on the ship, either.  Nothing is worse than spending 3 or 4 colonists on a ship and then not getting it.  It's much too damaging to get in a bidding war and then lose.  Your whole turn is shot at that point.  Those are my big gripes.  I have to say this is one of the best Eagle/FF games lately, though.  I'd put it right behind RRT.

RATING: 3
There's no big things that I can say about why I don't like this game. It's just lots of little things that add up and gnaw away at my sanity. On top of that it's long. :(
It takes the caylus/pillars of the earth type of gameplay where you go around in order and put a guy down somewhere to pre-program the turn.
Depending on what players have done earlier, the players will have differing numbers of workers with different special abilities. Sounds interesting until you realize that everyone likes to keep these in their hands to avoid mixing them with unused pieces, and so you get to ask "how many guys do you have left?" for two hours, as this is always important.
There is an aspect of set collection, which has a telephone problem, since it feels like someone should stop someone from getting a large set. (3 furs come up on one turn and the first player goes to trade? Who's going to stop him?)
There's an aspect of area control which is easy to do once it gets to you, but impossible to plan ahead very far for.
On top of this, you get varying amounts of VP depending on what cards you flip up. but wait! you might not get it if you didn't blindly allocate enough resources.
Components:
Looks pretty, but the plastic figs are many times hard to distinguish. (how many guys do you have? uh, which ones of those are merchants?) There are building that do different things with the same picture, and building that do the same thing with different pictures. The score track is artistically obscured.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Ok. Forget the name. Alright, this game is an interesting worker placement/engine building game. I did alright on my first play, and found the game to be pretty accessible. 

Maybe on the edge of being too long for me, I'd definitely play again though. Also, I'm kind of burned out of heavy games with the area control central mechanic. Granted, I find that mechanic in this game to be extremely thematic, the idea is just tiresome to me.  

2nd Play: The length, and randomization of discovery are very disappointing to me. Why include such a luck factor in such an otherwise open information game? At best, this is a poorly playtested element of the game. At worse this game is broken for it. For a game with such a random element, this game is way too long. 

Don't be tricked by the multiple paths to victory idea here either, much like how you absolutely need a 3rd family member quickly in Agricola, you need to have a strong presence in the new world to win. So, yes there are many ways to get VPs in this game, but those are more or less secondary to getting guys in the new world. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5

Update 7-20-2015: Played this again recently after a pause of many years. Although there are some flaws in terms of balance and randomness, I still had a really good time. Up to a 6.5

RATING: 8
Ok... I had written off pretty much any game by Mr. Drover as one I wouldn't want to play.  After having heard from a gamer with the same opinions that this was one he was going to BUY, I played it that night.  (He buys very few games).

I was surprised.  This is a very good game with a psuedo-random element of building offerings / commodities available; but with the ability to 'manage' them.  The mechanics are quite good, and there is a lot here which could lead to many plays without a mechanical feel.  (I think those that disagree haven't investigated combat to its fullest potential... but this is not a 'wargame'.)

I do dislike the scoring track (difficult to see at places, and very small); and the bright, plastic pieces can be tricky to differentiate at a distance.  (If I was to acquire, I would paint the bases similar colors to compensate)

I have acquired, but have not done any paiting.  The Euro crowd as moved on, and with a new addition to the family; I haven't the time atm.

RATING: 7.5
I do well at this game which I attribute to the fact that I'm a generalist gamer by nature, and this game rewards the "little bit of everything" approach. Those people who want to specialize in one way and hammer it home are naturally frustrated if the capstone to their strategy (i.e., the right capitol building or certain goods) doesn't arrive. I argue they're going about it wrong. This is more Agricola than Le Havre.

For discoveries, you go with five until all the 5s are gone; otherwise think of it as the known cost to explore and don't risk going with four dudes. You might get hosed relative to other tiles, but you'll get something that's worth VPs and some money. Just don't make it the heavy thrust of your strategy. For the goods, recognize that you'll have to use a turn when you're high up in the turn order to secure a chocolate boat (as we like to call them) to complete a set. Do that twice at least and you're good.

The game handles 5P very well and turns are lightning quick. There are different paths to make points, and the game forces you to constantly adapt rather than doggedly pursue a strategy you had chosen on turn 1. For me, whatever weak points the game has are far outshined by the game's strengths. Highly recommend to anyone interested in worker placement.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Rules Complexity: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Luck Factor :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::star:
Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Player Interaction: :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:

This game lives up to the hype.  It really does remind me of a cross between Puerto Rico (buildings), El Grande (area majority), and Caylus (worker placement).  AOE is different enough from Caylus to warrant having both, but I prefer Caylus because of its perfect information.  My only minor gripes with this title are: 1) the luck required when facing the natives on a discovery mission 2) the wide variety in the buildings' worth and 3) the poor quality of the box with no insert to boot.

RATING: 10
Played my first game Saturday with five players. As said before, you can't do everything but even when you're 'blocked' there are usually other quite good options available. The first go round of capital building purchases should 'urge' you into one of the strategies for victory. (1 free: captain/soldier/missionary/merchant/colonist per turn).Learn how to acquire money each and every turn in order to purchase capital buildings (CB) ideally at least every other turn IMO.I was able to win by getting the Privateer CB ($1/my ship/per other players/turn) and then the Navy CB (3 (?) VP/ship) and the Power CB (2 VP/soldier in the colonies) at the end (five ships and 6 or so soldiers in the colonies. (I started by getting the Inca CB ($20 immediately). None of my CBs had any end game VPs.I only even attempted discovery once in the entire game - in the last turn from the cards (successfully) - so I only received 6 or 7 (?) VPs from discovery.Others thought that the Navy CB was too powerful - of course I begged to  differ ;-) - but no one seriously was attempting to win the ships each turn.The next game with experienced players may not permit such a path to victory.One player would prefer a 4 player game next time and tracking victory points on the scoring track as the CB's are purchased and colonies once there are at least 3 colonists. I liked doing it as in the rules - players can alway count 'em up as most everything is public knowledge.

RATING: 6
If you're looking for anything like the computer game, look somewhere else. There is some thematic overlap but otherwise the connection is ephemeral at best.

AoE is a worker placement game. One of the available options is sending your people to the New World where you try to gather victory points through majorities. This is probably the most controversial part of the game since it introduces the only significant element of luck, and it can be quite considerable here. The rules aren't overly complicated, and the game is certainly not the monster of complexity you might expect from the box and the components.

Speaking of the components, the board, while visually pleasant, is slightly dysfunctional. It has some large empty areas while some of the areas where workers are placed would have benefitted from a little more room.

With regards to the gameplay, AoE doesn't bring anything new to the table. It does, however, have a certain appeal for crossover gamers because it's possible to wage wars against each other. Those skirmishes are entirely deterministic but direct conflict is at least unusual in your typical worker placement game.

RATING: 3
Age of Empires lives up to it's hype.  Multiple strategies are available to explore for victory, as well as just enough randomness in discovery draws, and capital buildings to ensure no two games play identically.  Placement of colonists/specialists present a similar feel to Caylus but Empires elevates beyond it by offering conflict, and elements of area control. Not to mention, the decision making is more dynamic as each turn and age changes the values of each option available significantly where as in Caylus there is often a sameness to each decision and very little arc in game play.  Age of empires has a nice balance and no one strategy seems too powerful (if all players are kept in check).  

Update:  Seems this is headed the same way as Caylus.  What initially was compelling, has really let down.  i don't think the engine is poorly designed, rather it seems to not inspire.  In contradiction to my previous posting I now believe the strategies are much more limited, and the replayability falls in line with this assessment.  I don't know what it is with this style of game (place workers), but the initial appeal dies quickly.  I am willing to play if requested but not without a yawn.
From an 8 to a 6.

Update: No desire to play this has dropped it to what it truly is in my opinion a mediocre game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
The stepchild of Caylus, Puerto Rico, and Mission: Red Planet, it manages to make an interesting synthesis of a range of mechanics without feeling like a jumbled mess. :) Nothing really new here, but it's fun without feeling like a clone.

I put far more weight on game design than production values, but in this case poor production directly interferes with gameplay several ways, so I find myself in the unusual position (for me) of lowering a game's rating because of its artwork and layout. The generally good quality components are marred by stupid, novice graphic artist mistakes. The scoring track is covered by decoration several places, making it hard to read. More obvious differentiation between the merchant and basic colonist would be welcome. Some of the long, narrow colonies in North America have created confusion, as the mass of settlers in them visually blend with their neighbors and make it unnecessarily difficult to see the borders and tell who is where. Meanwhile there are vast empty areas on the board, and it's clear that those colonies were squeezed to make space for a stupidly oversized logo, which could have gone in some area that was already empty -- like the mid-atlantic -- if it was so bloody important.

The box is flimsy and damages easily, but the pieces are durable enough.

The balance of a few capital buildings is also "suspect."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I was very pleasantly surprised with this one.  Interesting gameplay throughout.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I don't know about you, but I'm getting pretty sick of this cookie-cutter "Pillars of Caylus da Vinci" genre.  You know the type: "Today, our store has the following 25 items for sale.  Please line up your errand boys and select one item each.  Then you will have the pleasure of using your items to swap around money and goods and victory points and manpower and infrastructure and . . . ."  "Ooh look honey, this store has majority control, too!.  Wow, it's just like you said - Age of Empires III really is the Wal-Mart of board games!"  I can see the appeal of Age of Empires III, and I would definitely play again if someone else suggested it.  There is a good deal of tension involved in timing and prioritizing your limited actions, and the bounty of actions available keeps the game interesting, allowing several different approaches to the game.  But I'll probably never play the game enough to see whether these strategic paths are well balanced against each other.  There are just too many frustrating aspects of the game (even after I've made allowances for the haunting "been there, done that" feeling that permeates everything).  The board is very ugly, the victory point track is nearly useless, the territories of the New World are poorly drawn and shaped, and the pieces themselves are ridiculous - unwieldy, easy to knock over, and much too difficult to tell apart at a glance.  The boom-or-bust "discovery" system also bothers me.  I would prefer "draw two, discard one," but would that make the discovery tiles/cards too strong?  With better materials and graphic design, I might have considered buying this game.

RATING: 8.8
Even though there are only 8 rounds to play you get more done than in Puerto Rico or Cuba (they feel that they end too soon). Also it offers variable turn order which is a must for such get your engine running and optimization games. The mechanics work well but then I have some ergonomic issues with the map:
Who the heck decided to print such a big and pointless AoS III logo onto the map? Leave it away and scale the new countries a little bigger, so that all Colonists and Soldiers find place there.
Also one could have drawn some squares to place to capital buildings where to logo is. The map is so big but the space is used suboptimal.
Also, graphical ornaments should not cover the score track, which could be a little bigger as well.
When I first saw the ships I thought they're important to explore the new world; nope, they're just a joker for the trade goods... (so they could be realised as well as cardboard).
Normally, the folded map is the size of the box, but AoS has a slighty bigger box with no insert, so all the stuff floats around... but all these are minor issues. The game is fun  I rate it a solid 9 minus 0.4 for above mentinoed issues, which should have been discovered when playtesting. Plus 0.2 for the second edition that I own now.

RATING: 9
I really like worker-placement games, and this one is at the top of my list. RATING: 6.5
Not the type of adaptation that anyone who has played the computer game would expect, but it is a decent worker placement game with many strategic paths.

However, for a game of its weight, the randomness of discoveries is just too high. You have to commit your colonists before you know the number of natives you must match. Since you lose those colonists regardless of success or failure, you may waste a lot of them, whether you play it safe (by excess) or take even a small risk and fail. Also, except for the number of spaces in the "colonist dock", the game does nothing to scale to the wide range of player numbers.

The plastic miniatures are nice, but not distinctive enough. And the colonies may get a bit too crowded with them, thanks to the horrendous graphic design of the board, with a large unused area in the middle and too little space used for the map.

RATING: 7
Age of Empires 3 is a worker placement game with an appalling set of rules which I had to read through no less than SEVEN times before I finally got the game mechanics down pat.

Start of your turn, allocate your workers to various tasks.  Then activate the tasks once all players have finished allocating, sit back and see the results.  Repeat for eight turns and score victory points.  

Now why couldn't the manual be that simple?

RATING: 6
Rating after 1 play with 5.
Fairly standard worker placement and area control euro mechanics hiding behind an Ameritrash presentation. Everything works alright, the differentiation on types of workers specialising in certain areas is a nice touch. Area control largely uninspired, but works well enough. Becomes a little unclear whose workers are in what province. I enjoyed that the first available buildings strongly suggest several tactics to you, giving a definite focus among the myriad options for the first few turns. I understimated the importance of cash to buy Era III vp buildings, and they seem to provide the majority of scoring boost (and perhaps seem a little unbalanced). Overall, conveys the theme well without any unpleasant mechanics, and plays in the right length to give a semi-epic Civ type game. Would play again, but would pick other games over this.

RATING: 8
Great euro-game with a strong theme.  The components are good, though I wish the pieces were easier to distinguish.  The different strategies you can take are varied, and the capital buildings really help them along.  The whole thing works together really well, and the turns flow by pretty fast, with the exception of scoring turns, where some points maximization occurs in the new world, slowing things down if you play with analysis paralysis prone players.  The mathing is obnoxious, but it shouldn't take very long.  The game is so good in so many ways that this one issue doesn't detract from it much.  We played with 4 players, but I can see it working for more or less.  I've also played a 6 player game that worked fine though you had to spend more time doing the "it's your turn"

UPDATE: Played again.  The theme is strong for a game with such simple rules, there are a lot of different strategies to take, it has great production values, and although it is a long game, it goes by fast.  This game is deserving of most of the praise it gets.  I will probably buy the rumored reprint.

UPDATE II:  Played two more times.  Four games of this, and I still feel like there are a lot of strategies I haven't tried.  What a great game!  Even people that just dabble in a bit of everything seem to do pretty well if they are dabbling efficiently.  

I am not ready to pronounce the soldier/warfare strategy dead yet.  I think the key to the strategy is to get an early income stream, (merchant tile or 5$ tile), get the free soldier tile, and then ignore everything else in the game except going to the new world and killing shit.  Turn order and capital buildings that help you are a bonus, and any leftover guys that can discover are a bonus.

RATING: 8
Despite looking like another over produced empty shell like Age of Mythology, despite a name that sucks, this is an *excellent* game mixing worker placement, auction, ressource management, area majority and a little war into a coherent whole while managing to stay middleweight in terms of complexity.
Mechanically it is an euro game, but it adds a good part of solid theme and table talk.
Thematically, it fits the same itch as Struggle of Empires , but it is more elegant, moves at a brisker pace and even though Struggle is a fine game, I would always propose  Age of Empires III instead for the shorter playing time.  
I hate the Incan gold tile; I dislike any card/tile that forces other players to react in a scripted manner or loose the game, so I remove Incan Gold alltogether.

RATING: 7.5
I was surprised by this one, as you have to make difficult choices most of the time.  All the mechanics appear to work well together, and they fit the theme.

There are multiple ways to win, and adapting your plan to maximize you every action is key.

The game board, counters and cards are nice.  The miniatures look good but are a tad too colorful (some people will have trouble with the green and blue).  The coins are simply awesome!!

On the down side, the point track around the board could have been done much better.  Why not stop at 100 points and have bigger and wider areas?  As it is, you're better to go to paper, which is too bad.

Would have rated this one a '8.5', but the small and narrow point track drops it to an '8'.

Says on the back of the box that it's "Expandable to six players"...  So when will the 6th player expansion come out?

RATING: 5
2-28-10
I don't much care for this game.  There is too much randomness for how long it takes (3+ hours).  Discoveries are stupid for this reason.  We had a guy who sent out 5 units each time to ensure he'd get the points, and most of them only needed 3 or 4 guys to do, effectively wasting his effort.  But then another guy was able to chance it the last turn with just 1 unit and happened to pick up a discovery that only needed 1.  Stupid amounts of secret randomness that seem completely unnecessary and feel very counter-intuitive.

Then the buildings are nice and random too.  Not the predictable randomness in well-done worker placement games like Agricola, but just complete 'throw them out randomly' with no way to tell what order.  That and they are way too powerful, and the only thing to sink money into.  Get money early, get big buildings later and dominate.  It's that easy.

Warfare is stupid - being limited to 10 soldiers ruins any chance of a conquest victory, since you simply cannot attack enough people.  I took two buildings to give plus soldiers and ran out by the last turn.  They seem much better suited to discovering, since money is king in this game.

The strategies you would expect to help you out become weak and thin and they're all pretty even.  There is no way to stop anyone else's strategies very much so it's just a race for money and points.  Worker placement done in a very weak way with way too much randomness for how long the game takes.  No agonizing decisions as it's all pretty clear what is open and what you'll be doing, no brain burning, not much fun, just generally mediocre.  I'd rather play just about any other worker placement game.

[ENG]

It looks like an Ameritrash game, but you play it like a Eurogame. It´s not so original, but I find it much more enjoyable than the games which it comes from (Puerto Rico, El Grande, Caylus). It has a good theme that permeates to the rules doing a very intuitive game. It rewards the long term thinking and you can find a huge variety of strategies. You can play it fast (no more than 120' with 4 players) an I do love all these miniatures, but not the gameboard design (there´s no place for the miniatures in some of the regions). It combines three mecanisms that I love: area control, worker placement, resource managment.

RATING: N/A
#1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Worker placement:Set collection:AC:Economic:War(slight)

Yes another derivative VP collecting game BUT alas another quite good one that is well themed and stands on its own very well. Some aspects from PR & Caylus with area control and a slight war effect thrown in make for a nice overall package.

Worker placement is the central mechanic which will net you all your actions in the game. Actions consist of: Changing turn order, colonizing (bringing units onto map), taking goods, buying buildings, discovering new land, training special units & war. You can also try to win the ship by the way of majority which will count as a "wild" good towards your sets of goods. There are 8 rounds with scoring at the end of round 3, 6 & 8, this works well and gives you time to build yourself up before scoring takes place. There are several paths here to pursue for trying to max out points and this is one of the games strengths.

There are some random elements here to help keep it fresh, goods and buildings which are not too hampering since you still have the power to choose but it also introduces a large luck element of being in the "right place at the right time" aka first in the turn order when the strongest ones come up. (this could be tweaked in that all are face up and known for every round) For these reasons turn order remains very important throughout and having the ability to change it was an important inclusion. Also random are the discovery tiles and cards, these can be a big blow if you get burned as you lose your whole investment (house rule here lose half rounded up?) so we have found it is safer to err on the side of caution and go discovering with at least 4 men (although you can still be burned by a not-so-common 5 doing this). Payouts on the discoveries also vary and is an unknown factor and although money is VERY important in the game it isn't too critical in these discoveries. 

The war effect while very slight was well implememtned having soldiers being able to shoot if you choose war as an action, also soldiers taking each other out first was a good design choice here I think for theme and balance. 

A pretty good design overall if not a little too random but that could be tweaked by 'gamers' that don't like it, everything works quite well together and the game flows smoothly. No downtime or AP which is a HUGE plus for this type of game and should clock in around 2 hours once familiar. Probably best with 4 for time, competitiveness level and control but appears quite scalable especially 3 to 5, 6 seems to be a bit of a stretch here though with no changes to the board but I would like to try it anyway. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
