RATING: 5
Pros: Multiple avenues to success, gorgeous (although not spacious enough in the right areas) board and components, nice tension and tough decisions. Fun theme.  Cons: Kingmakery and way too lucky for the time it takes to play it. I'm disappointed, because I like a lot of what this game has to offer; but the luck element dooms it for me. I don't like playing 150 minutes and have a game decided on a card flip (4 VPs are meaningful) or a last turn, "I'll attack... that guy."  Yes, positioning and strategy can alleviate *some* of this--the age old question, "what do you want from a game?"  The game is fun enough and I probably wouldn't turn down a game if it's what everyone else insisted on playing. There's a bit of Puerto Rico here--you can pursue different strategies, but for any given game there's a certain best one depending on what everyone else is doing. We played in a fairly fast group, so I see danger of this dragging. This is a game that risks one of my major pet peeves--not thinking on other people's moves. Know what you want to do beforehand, have an alternate in mind if that's blocked, and throw in an "I'll just do this then" as a last resort.     BGG rank as of 7/11/2007:  31.  Huh?

RATING: 10
Puerto Rico meets Conquest of the Empire.  I believe this will be held up as the standard for hybrid design:  a Eurogame with Ameritrash sensibilities.  The first Glenn Drover game I purchased was Sid Meier's Civilization (awful rules, boring play), then I bought Attack! (tedious), then despite swearing off Eagle Games I purchased Conquest of the Empire, which quickly became our gaming group's favorite (using Drover's rules, rather than Larry Harris' rules).  Clearly, Drover's designs have improved with experience:  Age of Empires III fixes a few of the issues I had with Conquest of the Empire (too few actions, too many special cards) and repeats great features like wonderfully authentic plastic coins.  Age of Empires III is one of the best board games by an American designer.  After playing it, I have no desire to ever play Puerto Rico again, as Age of Empires is truer to its theme and less abstract.  Plus, it has plasticky goodness!

RATING: 7
As a design, this game is probably worth an 8 to me.  Not as finely tuned as Caylus, but with more distinct options and special powers.  At first glance it looks like multiple strategies are feasible, which is always a plus in a Euro-style game.  I'd definitely play again, though I did feel like it was taking longer than I wanted it to.  Personal note:  one of the special conditions which actually makes me enjoy area-majority is a special power that gives me lots of extra tokens.  I don't think missionaries are the strategic be-all, end-all of the game, but they're probably the only way I can have fun at it.

However, the components, while very pretty, are less than fully functional.  The plastic pieces are difficult to tell apart at first (colonist/merchant, captain/soldier), but this becomes clear after an hour or so of play.  The money is slippery, which wouldn't be as much of a problem if not for the lack of denominations other than 1 and 10.  Another player had the capital building that gave him one unit of income for each merchant ship.  My base income being 11, I was far more annoyed at having to handle eleven slippery 1-value coins per turn so I could hand over three of them than at losing my purchasing power.  The board is worse.  Given how many plastic pieces are crammed into each region, the regions are far too small.  A few "big colonists" which represent three or five, as Pillars of the Earth uses, would help.  Finally the scoring track is simply awful.  Certain scoring spaces are obscured by superfluous "discovery" images, and the spaces are too small for the scoring markers, which being regular plastic colonists do not stack.  Seriously, the game already has a huge amount of plastic pieces - I doubt five more nicely rendered stackable scoring disks would have blown away the cost structure for a fifty-dollar game.  I strongly suspect we got the score wrong for at least one of our five players, and in a game which came down to two points, that is a big deal.

I usually care far more about function than form and don't like to downgrade titles because of their components.  These components actually interfered with my enjoyment of the game.

RATING: 5
First impressions are usually hard to shake... here's mine after one play with 4 players.

There seems to be just too many problems with the game... both in design and in play balance. 

Why, in a game about exploration of the new world, is the new world map so damned tiny?  Once a large number of pieces exist, it's tough to tell who is in what region (the pastel colours don't help either).  It seems that the 'action selection' portion of the board is larger than it needs to be, and the new world map is too small.  And there's no place to put the trade goods or building tiles!

The initiative selection is counter intuitive... it's the first action performed, but the turn order doesn't change until the end of the round.  What's up with that?  It should be resolved right away!

A player with an early supply of money or soldiers is going to win... I don't see how others can compete.  Even a single soldier early in the game can provide someone a commanding lead.  A 'runaway leader' in this game is very tough to catch.  I call it the Löwenherz syndrome.

Left-Right Binding is also of major importance in AoEIII.  Sometimes you have to react to the player on your left to the detriment of any other plans you might have had.  If all you do after the first couple of turns is "react to the leader," the game's fun factor drops fast!

I'm sure that the game has been playtested but, for some reason, it still feels "rough" to me.  It's not a bad game, and I'll probably play again, but I doubt that I'll bother buying a copy for myself.

Needs a few house rules to help balance the game.  Allowing multiple purchases of certain tiles/buildings would be a good start... and playing with open discoveries would help too.

I'll give this one a "take it or leave it" rating of '5.'

RATING: 5
A pretty good marriage of theme and gameplay undermined by the randomness of several key elements. The disparity in value between buildings and discoveries is appalling given the total lack of control the players have over which ones they can acquire. As other commenters have noted, this - and the randomness of the trade good draw - takes most of the planning aspect out of taking the initiative; you're at the mercy of the tiles that appear when you decide to do so. It's also unclear how a player who falls behind early can hope to recover. The best buildings add pieces to a player's available stock, which translates into more actions per turn, which means more ways to score and to acquire the money that you need to buy additional buildings. Although nicely self-contained, it means an early setback can be devastating. For a game that takes 3  hours to play, that level of unpredictability is unacceptable.

RATING: 7
Rating based on about 6 plays thus far; unlikely to rise to an 8, may eventually fall to a 6.  Don't get me wrong, this is a very solid game that borrows heavily in its mechanics from some of the very best Euros and it fits the theme nicely.  On the positive side, there appear to be multiple paths to victory, and the four types of scoring (from buildings, discoveries, income generation and colonists) appear to be more balanced than, for example, the four favor tracks in Caylus.  On the negative side, however (and these factors prevent me from giving the game a higher rating of say 8 or 9), there is the huge luck factor on discoveries (something that can be amelioriated with a couple of house rules, but in my mind an annoying flaw in the game system)-- and on the components end, while the plastic pieces and the coins are very nice, the scoring track (with some numbers totally obliterated in favor of some questionable and very impractical artwork) is an abomination.  So, while this may be the flavor of the moment (as I post this in early July of 2007), I seriously doubt this one will be receiving nearly as much play in another year or even six months down the road.  Of the newer games, I rate it a cut behind Imperial, Through the Ages, Leonardo and Pillars which for me are the still the four best of the past year, although this one is not bad.

Ratings update:  Up to around 12-15 plays now, and I would have to say this game is a very solid 7, would be 7.5 if I gave 1/2 point rankings.  There appears to be no killer strategy that dominates, each game is different mainly because the order of buildings is different each game, plus the four ways of scoring seem fairly well balanced, as witnessed by several really close games played by experienced players.  The only downside is the tremendous luck range on the discovery tiles; if this wasn't part of the game, it might check in at an 8 or even a 9 rating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2
No, its not ameritrash, but it is not eurotrash either.  Its Drover's umpteenth attempt to marry euro and ameritrash together - until now the attempts have been at best modest successes and mostly horrible failure.  However, AOE3 is actually a lovely mix of some abstraction while still retaining thematic depth.  Most surprising is the 'flow' of the game - it really moves easily and quickly.  Its all about allocating 'labour' to the various tasks involved in conquering the new world - missionaries, soldiers, ship captains, merchants and plain old settlers.  Yes, it is 'worker placement', but the 'workers' mostly stay on the table, now playing the area majority role.  I notice that many of the naysayers glibly note that AOE3 is simply worker placement plus area majority, but the way these two aspects work together is really underappreciated - its NOT an obvious 'evolution' of the worker placement genre and Drover needs to be commended for it.  The 'trade goods' part is a bit underdone but its tolerable.  There are agonising decisions but turns are short so AP should not be a big problem.  A little disappointing that the military strategy is not more potent, but it CAN be important.  The game works fine for any number of players from 2 to 5, but with 2 it is a bit dry and unexciting.  Update:  played more and raised rating to 9 this game is simply good - really does play fast and smooth, and the theme is very well handled.  Its a funny thing - there are a lot of games out there which are technically better designed (Agricola, Princes Of Florence, Goa etc) but I would much rather play this!  Note that you should use the auction variant for deciding starting turn order.

RATING: 7
Disappointing lack of tension in the game.  The competition for event boxes doesn't seem very intense, and if in doubt just stick workers in the Discovery box for later on.  Competition for discoveries is also weak since most of the time no one knows quite what they're aiming to discover.  There's little incentive to risk a discovery with few colonists because there's no way of assessing the likelihood of success (unless a prior one happens to have failed), so everyone just makes sure they have five before they attempt to discover.  This element of randomness is the weakest part of the game.  I still enjoy playing it, but for worker placement, Agricola and Caylus are *much* better.

Designet af Glenn Drover, dette brætspil version af PC hit, Age of Empires III fra Microsoft og Ensemble Studios, giver dig mulighed for at gense en alder af udforskning og opdagelse. Tag rollen som en kolonimagt, der søger berømmelse, ære og rigdom i Den Nye Verden. Som du fortsætter gennem tre tidsaldre, kan du starte ekspeditioner opdagelse, kolonisere regioner, udvide din handelsflåde, bygge Capitol bygninger, som giver din nation klare fordele, udvikle din økonomi, og om nødvendigt, erklære krig.

RATING: 7.5
This game feels like a mish-mash of all games Euro. On the left you have an El Grande map (9 regions; all [6][2][0]). On the right, you have a Pillars of the Earth/Caylus sequential action track. 

The game is quite good, it plays quickly, fairly easy to learn if you're a Eurogamer, although the Income system was very unintuitive. I felt the actions were too limited, that players were forced into the Discovery action when there was nothing left to do. And even though there are multiple paths to victory, ultimately everyone just seems to get in each other's way (not because they WANT to, but because there isn't any other action left to take).

Components were great. Artwork for map was crap (it got really cluttered), but the plastic coins and well-sculpted pieces more than made up for it.

RATING: 7
Based on 5 plays.  AoE III is probably my favorite release of the past year.  It combines tactical maneuvering with a broad strategic scope in an excellent way.  Each turn confronts you with an array of challenging decisions.  The game runs a little long with 5, but I'm happy to play even with that number.  The only real complaint is that luck manifests itself in a few unpleasant ways.  I wish discovery had been handled with a little less chance, and that you could count on the appearance of certain Capital Buildings (esp. the victory-point-laden ones from Age III).

UPDATE:
Well, favorite game of 2007 is perhaps a little generous.  Though entertaining, the game's length and the swings of luck from the Discovery tiles make it harder to get to the table than I might like.  I'm looking forward to the designer's next game, which supposedly implements aspects of this one.

RATING: 8
Epic and probably the best game experience I've ever had.

A real feeling of progression as the game continues.

A wonderful combination of several game systems and I would rather play this game than the ones I feel it combines (Puerto Rico, Caylus, Antike).

Many layers to win, but all in all not that complex to teach.

I told myself no more big box games, but I will be making room for this one and (hopefully) expansions.

Miniatures can be a bit tough to distinguish and the map can be hard to read once covered in miniatures.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good exploration game with set collection and area majority mechanisms dipped in a mild sauce of straightforward conflict (resolved without dice!). Try to find the optimal mix of capital buildings, discoveries and colonist majorities in the New Land. Make sure to build up an economy (via trade goods and merchant ships) and to send colonists to the new world (missionaries are extremely handy to take additional trade goods and establish a firm presence). Discoveries are useful for additional cash and victory points. Capital buildings are crucial to get extra cash and specialists at the beginning of the game and for additional victory points at the end of the game. What's not to like? Well, as minor points: there is a bit too much chrome (I would prefer wooden cubes instead of plastic miniatures) and the scoring track is horrible (some of the fields are hidden by redundant art). **** Major concern after our first play: the game might be too unbalanced as some of the capital buildings look very powerful (the randomly determined startplayer can get very lucky with the initial selection - I was rather glad I could grab the trade post in my first play & as another example, having a capital building giving victory points for trade goods might be overkill as these provide already victory points). Other combo's or skillful play through other victory point mechanisms might counterbalance this, but this was not really the case in our first play [five players] with a very clear runaway leader rather early on (very relaxing if you are in the driving seat :-)). **** The second play [four players] resulted in a much more balanced endscore, although again some of the combo's for the capital buildings are really powerful and the chance element of which capital buildings become available might wreck a strategy aimed at certain capital buildings with a high return on victory points. **** The balance in our third play [four players - all had played before] was OK. **** All sessions took about 4 to 5 hours to play.

RATING: 7.75
Pretty sure this is my brother in law's favourite game. I rate it highly although I prefer other worker placement games if truth be told. I still think it's a great game though and multiple paths to victory is always going to be a winner. However, there is an element of luck involved here. Some of it, namely the discovery tiles, you can choose not to get involved in- if you do participate, it is pretty galling to send 5 soldiers in to claim a 1 native tile before watching your opponent send 2 soldiers in to claim a 2 soldier tile. The other luck element is unavoidable however- the capital buildings in the 3rd round are randomly drawn and can offer up big VP bonuses in the end game. If the strategy/ strategies you took throughout the game are not rewarded by the correct buildings coming out, you're probably in trouble.

RATING: 7.5
[2007.Sep.15] A well-themed, analytical game from the same mold as Caylus and The Pillars of the Earth [TPofE], although slightly closer to TPofE due to presence of a luck element (in the form of Discovery tiles/cards) and a fixed number of game turns (in this case 8 turns). Some minor gripes include: (a) the oversized, insertless game box, (b) the non-German-style game board folds (creases), (c) the strangely shaped New World regions that can become over-crowded with colonists, and (d) a lack of on-board zones for the current turn's active buildings and trade goods (the board definitely has enough space for such). All-in-all minor (component-based) gripes that barely mar a good game.

:star::star::star::star::nostar:

RATING: 7
An in-depth worker placement game that takes a bit of a different turn from others in its class.  Instead of collecting rocks and wood and food, you're placing your colonists, merchants, missionaries, soldiers and captains to create new workers of those types, buy buildings, earn income and sail off to new lands.  The first round (of eight) had me a bit confused.  By round two I was good to go and enjoyed the game thoroughly.  Even with a fourth place finish (out of four people), I can honestly say this game was a lot of fun once you get your bearings.

RATING: 7
I was surprised when one of my friends bought this one and asked me to read the rules to play it some time later. It is quite entertaining and the pieces are beautiful.

The fightning seems useless at first, but after the first game was won because of *one* fight (by 2 points!), the whole mechanics made sense and the strategic deepness became evident. 

This is how Pillars of the Earth should be.

Trust me, I'm not a big fan of miniatures in this kind of game, in fact I would play this with meeples and it would still be a fulfilling experience, but still the miniatures add that something else.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After the success of [GAMEID=31260], we were looking for another heavier worker placement game.  It probably isn't as hard as [GAMEID=31260] to pick up, since there's fewer possible actions and they're mostly the same each round, although there are more specialized workers to place.  There also seems like quite a few paths towards victory, either going for discovery, colonization, resources and buildings, or even military.

However, the game seemed to lack a little something with just two players.  It feels like it's missing that "tenseness" and "turn angst" that [GAMEID=31260] had.  The gameplay also feels a little too wide open---there doesn't feel like a lot of interaction between the players and it's easy to go ahead with your strategy.  It's easy to acquire a lot of workers to place, so usually you're able to do what you need.  The colonization space is the only one that's dramatically limited in the two player game with only three available spots.  With the limited colonization opportunities, it's harder to steal a colony from under someone's nose and the military path seems limited as well, since you just can't get enough soldiers into the New World.  With only two players, you also miss out on the possibility of shifting alliances between different players.  

Also, I have to admit that I prefer wooden components over the plastic components here.  We mistook what each piece was far too often...

Update:  we tried this with three players and the game felt completely different.  However, it made us appreciate the differences from the two-player game---the competition switches to different actions.  With more players, it's a lot more difficult to get resources and extra specialists while the colonization actions are much tighter with two.  We've gone back to play with two and found it more fun now.  Maybe it's because we're more familiar with the game now, but while at first it felt like an open game of abundance, it's actually a very tight game and you have to choose the best actions at the best times carefully.  Recommended with two.

RATING: 9
A very robust system which allows the game to play smoothly, a logical narrative, clear signposting of the cause and effect of actions,  several paths to victory, excellent components and a "snowball" point scoring system (1st round only a small number of victory points are available compared to the later rounds when the snowball has gained momentum rolling down the hill) which allow you to feel yourself into the game and are not consigned to failure if you make a wrong decision in the early round.

An excellent game, with high replay value, which because of the positives listed above I would have no hesitation to introducing to newbies or gamer alike.

RATING: 8
Played with 4 and with 2. This is a very good game. Lots of thing to track, different paths to victory. It's important to balance money (to get those damn building) and colonist (to get pts). I like the Discovery mecanism but I think it is very easy to get screwed in pts (sacrifying lots of colonists for a cheap discovery). Maybe this would be better with open discovery tile?

The only strange thing is Warfare. With 2 players it seems to be a very good strategy to battle your opponent as often as possible (especially if you have soldier advantage). It's easy to do because usually you have a lot of money to spend. With 4 players though... this is seldom used. Money is so tight that you don't want to sacrifice buying a building to go to war.

All in all, a good game with a nice theme that doesn't feel pasted on!

RATING: 7
This is by far the best design to come out of the "Drover" line.

The board is a bit too large, but then again, presentation does make this game stand out.

Fantastic bits, wonderfully clear rules (they give a brief overview then a detailed description, and even have an advanced variant for starting position), and a collection of familiar mechanics masterfully blended into what makes a fantastic game.

So many avenues to victory, and a wealth of synergy makes this one almost a 10...but more plays will prove that.

Update:

This one settles at a solid 7. In time other designs have appeared which do similar things either better or in a shorter play time. This game still does some unique things on it's own and the worker placement style is still not totally usurped. It certainly retains it's spot in my collection.

RATING: 9
I really like this game and will play it almost any chance I get as it is a lighter version of Dominant Species.  You place different types of workers to acquire various resources like money, other workers, ships to make sets of goods, goods to give you more money and buildings.  You place all your workers out and resolve in the order they were placed from top to bottom.  You score through area control on a map where you have the most colonists an soldiers.  You can also scope by discovering new colonies for settlement.  The mechanics are nicely balanced and the expansion is fun.  The only minor problem was that certain versions of the game and a poor score track and the miniatures looked similar but there are nice files on the geek to help make this easier to play.  

Not really a family game, but easy to teach.  I will almost always play this game if suggested.

RATING: 7
+ 
- (too) balanced game ; difficult to say in advance who is in the lead, even not in the last round ! Nobody is never locked out of the game. This gives always a positive social athmosphere on the gaming table.
-perfect and clear game system without exceptions ; easy to explain to non gamers

- good game duration ; 30 min/  player 

-

You have not a perfect control of the apparition of the buildings, so it's difficult to follow a single long term plan, but in practise this consideration is not so important, you simply take what the game gives. These negative things are only small issues. 

It's a relatively short but very enjoyable game.

RATING: 9

RATING: 10
Description:  This game was compared to five different games by the five different players at the table.  Suffice it to say that many mechanics are at work:  resource management that feels at times like bidding, at times like wargaming, at times like area control.  There are many paths to victory (bringing the inevitable comparisons to PR) but less importance in seating position because of the initative track.  I felt like it was everything I like about Princes of Florence with the distasteful stuff distilled out.  Not quite as good as El Grande, but maybe I need more plays before I can rightfully compare it to one of my favorites.

Negative:  we had one gamer who is particularly prone to AP.  Everyone has a different theory about WHY he is AP'ed, but the fact remains that he singlehandedly added about 90 minutes to the playtime of this game.  And most of it was in the placement from the colonists ship in turns 6 and 8.  One of the other players actually left before the scoring - he was "fed up", and even though I enjoyed the game (and a victory!) I was fed up as well.  Sadly, I think I will have to avoid playing with the AP'er (even though I enjoy plenty of other games with him) when it comes to this one.

UPDATE:  Four players was an even better experience.  The game moved, but remained quite tense and three players had a chance at the end.  This may be on its way to a 10 rating.

I can't imagine having fun with 6 players playing this.

UPDATE:  We played a five player game, but all players had at least one game experience.  Also, we had made a gentlemanly agreement to take our turns more expeditiously.  We finished the game in less than 2 hours.  It was quite fun and tense... I'd built a strong early lead with heavy population in the New World.  However, Jay played a strong financial strategy and built a large bankroll. He was able to acquire lots of capital buildings in the last turns and pull to a tie.  I missed two discoveries in turn 7 and 8... had I just waited to have six explorers, instead of taking two chances with four, the VP's would have made a hugh difference.  Also, Jay had a couple soldiers and was able to influence another player's options for warfare simply by intimidation... I had no soldiers on the board.  Final score?  Jay 94, Mac 93 with the other players scoring 79 or less.  The agony!!!

UPDATE: Another 5 player game. Again, we got done in less than 2 hours. Perhaps I'm paranoid, but it seemed three players looked for conflict with me in the first age. I couldn't wreak vengeance on them all, so I focused on the player who hurt me the most by taking away a set of trade goods and a colony from me. The "vengeance" strategy (as many soldiers as possible) actually worked pretty good, incidentally. I ended up in second... again losing to Jay. Jay 95, Mac and xLx 86.

UPDATE:  Five player.  Again, I went with an aggressive soldier strategy.  I was able to pay expenses with Incan Gold, an early set of tobacco, 4 and 5 in initiative, and an early Merchant.  I got my soldier from training, until the Age II soldier capital building.  By the end of Age II I secured 26 points of colonies, and no one was interested in challenging... I had one or two soldiers in each!  Indian Allies and the migrating soldier buildings help me add muscle.  Although I didn't get the Power building, I was able to hold my 26 points of colonies and add 13 economy, 8 discovery (with two soldier expeditions!!) and 11 capital building points.  Final score Mac 90, Jay 86, Boaz 79.

UPDATE:  AoE III... you deserve it.  10!!!

UPDATE - GAME SIX:  Four player game, and I again went with the soldier strategy.  I got hugely lucky with my discoveries (needed/sent) - 4/4, 3/3, 5/4 and 6 VP discovery with New World Cartograpgy.  I got 14, 20, and 26 VP's from colonies in the scoring rounds, and got "Glory" with 10 soldiers on the board.  Notably, the other players started taking away my opportunities to obtain soldies in round 4, but I already had 6 on the board at that point.  Final score: Mac 117, Yoel 107.

UPDATE:  Game SEVEN, 5 players.  I get the soldier capitol building early, then focus on the New World, trade goods, and ships.  I pick up three ships on the cheap, and end with 3x4 sets and 1x3 set (I drew some lucky tiles with West Indies).  I get 4 discoveries, 3 were gambles on tiles with 3 discoverers (1,2,2 natives defending).  My final tally is balance with 21 VPs economy, 18 discoveries, 21 capital buildings, and 2,16,20 from colonies.  Mac 98, Boaz 87.

UPDATE:  5 players.  The luckiest game I ever played.  I had 6 trade goods, 2 sets of three with 2 ships.  I had 8 Soldiers in the new world, and stayed away from the only other player with Soldiers.  I got Incan Gold early, and had 5 solid buildings in the first Age.  I maintained a cash flow in the middle with some trained Merchants.  I got lucky on the Age Three buildings, good for 27 VP.  In the end, I tallied 111, Jay 87, Larry 73.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Well-themed, with a good overall narrative arc and lots of opportunities for big and little strategies. After the one play, it appears to be very well balanced - knowing more about the capital building that give VPs would even have increased the strategic appeal.

There are, however, some rough edges, which mostly resolve around scoring rounds: when landing colonists, the information available (i.e. who else will land colonists, warfare, ...) is fixed, so that it is possible to calculate VP outcomes, but the decision tree has enough branches to make this last a while. With the right players (or a timer), this should not cause much fuzz, but it is a well-paved way into analysis paralysis. And I'm not particularly fond of the boom-or-bust approach to expeditions, either.

That said, the game certainly is good, good enough that I'd very much like to play again.

Played once.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've played this three times now and liked it a little better each time.  That said, it still hasn't totally grabbed me.  It's like a blend of El Grande (area control) and Pillars of the Earth (pawn placement--not sure what this is really called, but you put your little dudes on a certain part of the board to acquire certain items, privileges, or actions that are doled out in area-order then dude-order).  You have to pick a strategy and let some options fall by the wayside, there are more ways to spend your dudes/money than you really need to utilize to make points; spreading yourself too thin by trying to do a little of everything is not the best way to go.  Played with four players twice and it flowed well until the last turn, when the area-control gets really hairy in anticipation of the final scoring.  Played once with six and the whole game dragged horribly, WAY too much down time.  I think the game does lend itself to AP with so many choices to be made on every turn.

RATING: 8
One of my favorites.

AOE III has nothing to do with the computer game other than it is about colonizing the new world...so be forewarned.

What it is though is a great worker placement game with tons of strategy and depth. The rules aren't that heavy and I have had an easy time teaching it to my group. The flow is great as it keeps everyone engaged the entire game. The components are great (save for the flimsy feeling box) and look the part (the coins sound nice clinking together).

Just all around a great package with lots of fun. I don't have a single gripe about this game.

RATING: 5.5
On my 2nd play, I almost got to finish the game.  I feel that it is about 30 minutes too long for what it is.  Basically, the game is Caylus with chrome and some randomness thrown in. And the chrome (namely the plastic minis) just adds length to the game as you constantly have to shuffle them around and they can be hard to distinguish. The other issue is that of turn order/placement. The game can get bogged down (especially with 5 players) because players don't go in clockwise order and each player can only place 1 colonist at a time.  Therefore,  you will lose valuable time constantly trying to figure out whose turn it is.  Also, the money tokens, while they look nice, don't stack very well.

Game play wise, it is a solid game, but offers nothing really new in the genre.  Is it different enough to stand out?  Maybe, but the length is an issue with 5+ players and I don't think the tension will hold up with fewer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I can see why this is a classic. There's so much to do, the tension is palpable, the bits are ridiculously overproduced and gorgeous, the art is great, and the overall experience is simply fantastic. I love the weight of the game. There's certainly a lot going on, and while it's by no means a "heavy" game, it's also far from light. It's a true medium weight game with many paths to victory. Like Endeavor, it has that "epic" feel to it. Speaking of Endeavor, a similarly themed game, I like both rather equally. Endeavor is lighter, I think and certainly quicker. AOE is better produced and possibly more cutthroat. It's also on par, in terms of rating, with Francis Drake. To be honest, I was on the fence whether this game was a 8.5 or a 9. The deciding factor was playing count. Against the advice of most users, I played this with only 2 the first time (my wife and I). It played awesome with 2! I'm sure it would be more tense and cutthroat with more, but with no variants whatsoever, the tension in a 2-player game can be easily cut with a knife. I love this game!

Update 2/8/15:

Played with 3 last night (Erin, Tyson and me). It was definitely better with 3 than with 2. It didn't seem like the board was that much "tighter" (credit to the 2-player experience), however, the warfare played a much greater role. After playing another game of this, I've seen some things I love and some things that I didn't particularly care for. 

Things I love:

-Art: beautiful colors and old world feel
-Components: love the minis, particularly the ships and the coins are quite nice
-Gameplay: The board makes the game feel epic! Also, even though you are sometimes placing 10 or more "workers" out on the board, your actions still seem scarce and important. This scarcity drives up the tension quite a bit. 
-Rounds: Being a Type A personality, I prefer having a set number of rounds in a game, and AOE does not disappoint. 
-Rules: They're not much to look at, but they are formatted approximately with a nice index. 

Things I didn't like: 

-The box. It's really bad. I have it sitting at the top of the stack to prevent dishing, but it's super thin cardboard and certainly not linen-finished. 
-The tiles: I'm not a fan of the art on the tiles. It's really blah. Also, I know it's a little difficult to put the entire action of each tile on a small counter, but looking the tiles up in the index constantly slows down gameplay in a game that is by no means short. 
-"Runaway leaderish": I wouldn't say that this game has a runaway leader problem, but it's close. I can say that it's unforgiving, particularly if you get left behind in exploration early on. Because of how the scoring works, it's a little difficult to make up lost ground. In a game of this length, you don't want it to be apparent that you have no way to win several rounds out. 

All in all, this game is truly great. For now, I'm going to keep it at a 9. Despite how much I like it, I cannot ignore the few problems I have with it. Nevertheless, this one will be in my collection for a long time.

RATING: 6
I've only played this once so far. It was ok but there was something missing. I assumed that this would be an empire building game and it isn't. The discovery aspect of the game wasn't satisfying for some reason. 

This game is basically a bidding game. You take your men and place them on one side of the board to try and get the resources you need. That's really the whole game. Everyone places their men in various boxes until they run out and then you find out who gets what. That's 99% of the game. It just felt dry.

I never count a game out after playing it once. I would like to play this again now that I have a better feel for it. Maybe I just wasn't in the mood for this type of game when I played it. Or perhaps I expected this game to be something that it wasn't which led to a little disappointment.

RATING: 7
Far better than I expected. It's a less nasty than a lot of worker placement games. In other worker-placement games (ie Caylus) only the first to place in a spot gets the action so it can be frustrating when someone else grabs a key spot for your strategy. Other such games (ie Leonarda da Vinci) give the action to whoever puts the most workers there - this can be even more annoying because some of your workers inevitably end up getting you nothing. For the most part this is not how worker placement in AoE works, so it's a more forgiving example of the genre.

RATING: 5
A game about standing in lines. First you stand in a line to see who gets to choose which line they want to stand in first, then you choose among different lines that will eventually let you do exciting things like discover a confined space (where you can later stand and get points), or buy buildings (which give you more people, meaning more opportunities to stand in more lines). Just make your selection and wait for your selection to arrive or be delayed, and then find another line to stand in.

Edit: played again with 4 (rather than 5), and it's more bearable. Raised rating from a 2.

RATING: 9
This game plays great with 3, 4, or 5 players.  Each has its own flavor.  You have to get 1st place in some of the colonies, but you cannot make dominating colonies your only strategy.

You have to adapt to the conditions on the board.  If a key trade good suddenly comes out, it becomes your number one priority.  Making sure you have enough money to buy a coveted capital building is also important.  Jumping up in the turn order at just the right time so you get first crack at the available buildings is also a great move.

This game has lots of dynamics and strategies.  I highly recommend it.

RATING: 4
It's not that bad of a game, and I'd play it now and then. Well, once a year, maybe, if there was nothing else available. Combat is too abstract for my tastes, and rules like "only one person per turn can buy a soldier/merchant/captain/missionary on a given turn"  don't make a lot of sense except as a typically Euroish balancing mechanism.  And it's just this sort of bizarre restriction that prevents Euros from being immersive. Eurogameland is the only land on Earth where there's only one ship captain for hire in the entire world.
I have some other minor issues (the territories are too small, for example), but overall it's a decent effort. The rules are simple enough to grasp during a first play (though not necessarily the long-term strategies).

This is right on the cusp of being a 5, very close. It would be for sure if it was shorter or if the combat was better or if it didn't have so much of that artificial Euro-ness to its mechanism. Eh, on second thought, it's not really that close to being a 5. Too long, too Euro, too much of all the things that make me shake my head in frustration at the design choices that detract from the immersive experience that I prefer.

RATING: 6
Kind of in-between Caylus and Pillars of the Earth, with the same basic main action selection system.
Pros: Very themeful, some tense decisions, several different strategies available for the win. I really liked the "combat" system and the integration of some sort of an area-control mini-game. 
Cons: Lots of randomness in the buildings; this can be especially frustrating in round 3 if the building you really were counting on for the win doesn't show up. Easily remedied through a simple house rule. Biggest problem however is that the game has very little game arc; the types of decisions to be taken change very little from round to round, making the game feel very static and kind of repetitive after round 3-4 (out of 8). This is the same problem that plagues Pillars of the Earth, and (to a much lesser extent) Caylus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I seem to have been playing a lot of games lately consisting of cobbled together euro mechanics.  This is no exception.  AoE III is a worker placement game where players user their workers for next-turn order, discovery voyages (for VPs, etc. but at a potential risk if you don't send enough workers), colonization (a mini area-control game), warfare (basically removing people from the colonies), goods (set collection for income), upgrading workers (powers), and buying buildings (Puerto Rico light).  

It all works well and has a theme that's easy for most people to relate too.  Some will find issue with the luck found in the discoveries (number of VPs, etc.), and tile draws for goods & buildings.   There's a huge issue with the first turn in that the first player can choose actions before anyone else and it's probably going to be to buy the best building.  Each subsequent player gets a pittance of one dollar per turn position after first.  Course that first player will then get the first pick of the second actions, etc. too.

I found the actions too derivative and the buildings to be somewhat uninteresting to rate this higher than a 7.  Additionally the aesthetics of the game really turn my stomach.

RATING: 3.9
This is my poster child for games where the components actively get in the way of playing the game.  The little plastic minis are oversized for the smaller regions of the board, they are impossible to tell apart when crowded together, they tend to fall over when jostled, and they just don't add anything.  

I assume that this game was not playtested in final production form.

Replace the plastic minis with wooden bits, redraw the map to replace some of the decorative chrome with colony regions that are actually large enough for a reasonable number of playing pieces (with visible boundaries), and (possibly) slightly re-balance the power of the buildings, and you'd have a cool game here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Picked up at B&N for 75% off on a whim - box lid was massively crushed.

First play, 4/18/09. Although this game shares mechanics with games like Pillars of the Earth, Stone Age, Agricola, and Caylus, this is the black-bearded half-uncle who disciplines you with an Iron Fist. Finally a euro-style worker placement game with bite! You can actually take WARFARE actions in this game. 

And indeed, in comparison to other games where there is military conflict, the conflict here is light, the key here is that there still IS conflict. LOTS of conflict. To clarify - the "conflict" in other worker placement games tends to be based on taking a valuable space before someone else has a chance. As such, it really does feel like, to use the cliche, multi-player solitaire. If you are careful, you can win games like that while completely ignoring the other players, simply by building the most efficient path to victory (and I've done it in Pillars of the Earth, Stone Age, and Agricola, so I know it's possible.) In AOEIII, however, the majority of the VP stand to be gained at the end of each age for majority control of colonies. The game actually encourages DIRECT conflict on top of the indirect conflict worker placement layer in that, one of the actions players can take is to ship soldiers and colonists to other players colonies, and later take WARFARE actions to fight for supremacy in those colonies. So right at the end of each age, there is a knife fight smack down fight for those VP's. For once, you can screw up someone else's VP gain DIRECTLY, instead of just taking actions that cut them off. 

Truly one of the better worker placement games out there.

============
TRADE INFO: First edition - Age of Empires III box, with smaller scoring track.
Bought this from the ding and dent table at B&N a while ago. Box has caving and a crushed corner. Contents in great condition - everything is sorted into plano boxes which I will include.

RATING: 8
Blind playtested four times with my play group and we are completely impressed with this game!!  I've played the computer game and there are similarities in the theme but game mechanics make this a 100% EuroGame (not even a wargame). 

Now that the final production version is out, I am rating it a 9.  This game does have, interestingly, bits and pieces of Puerto Rico (trade goods include Indigo, Sugar, Tobacco and Coffee (but there are 7-8 more type of goods as well), Caylus (places pieces one at a time in multiple action boxes in turn order) and Struggle of Empires (area control on a map with points for first and second) built in, but I would argue this game is somewhat of a mish-mash of mechanics as a result.

The game also has discovery, colonists, specialists (missionaries, captains, soldiers and merchants).  The game design is elegant and I think it will stand the test of time.

Very challenging to win and good for 2-5 players.  I like it as a solid 3-4P game, so far.  Even more involved and challenging as conflict and discovery increases with 5 players.  Won my first 5P game last night by one point and what a game!!  

With time we play less and less and I rarely choose it anymore so downgrading to an 8 as it is still a solid game

RATING: 7
This was my favorite game the latter half of my childhood. I love the art and the miniatures. The play style is intuitive and satisfying and all the mechanics make sense and are easy to grasp. It is a strategic game, but not critically strategic. 
There are a couple things that bother me. I wish combat was more of a necessity. You can really go a whole game without combat, which sort of makes soldiers useless as well as a couple capital buildings. Also, the merchants and captains are much stronger than the missionaries and soldiers. The missionaries only give you an extra person through the colonist dock. I think their power should also work if they survive a discovery. And soldiers are even more useless. In general, I like the right side of the board where you place all your peeps in trade goods cap buildings etc, but the left side of the board where you colonize the new world is a little underwhelming. It would be cool if you could move your people once a turn or something.Your people just arrive there and stay there for the rest of the game, that feels weird. A good and bad for me is that the winning strategy for this game really comes down to Age 3 capital buildings. 
Anyways, I still love this game. I have gotten new gamers to enjoy this game, but usually I need to play this with people who are up for a big board game. It takes at least 2 hours to play and takes significantly longer with new folks.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In this worker placement game of New World discovery, each player gets five colonists per round. You use them to get ahead in turn order, build, get trade goods for income, get specialized colonists like soldiers or merhcants, move them on a size limited boat to the colonies and conduct war. 

This is a very engaging game with lots of strategy. It is fresh, and very fun to play. Game length is around 3 hours. This game works quite well for 6 players as well. 

I like it since it is full of tension. You don't know if a player with soldiers is going to pay a visit to your colonists, and you don't want to be too far behind in turn order. There are a lot of things to think about.

There is one action where you do a discovery, sending colonists to conquer a colony. You have to defeat natives, which vary from strength 2-5, then later the cards vary from 2-6. There is a huge variance in the strength and VPs. One game, I did try to make this my whole strategy, which doesn't work. The variance of the tiles/cards might turn some people off, but you make discovery a small part of your strategy and you will have a more enjoyable game. In my case, when I do a discovery, I will send the maximum expected to ensure success (of course counting to see if those high numbered tiles/cards aren't exhausted already), rather than risk failure.

RATING: 5
Lots to like.  Questionable how much higher the rating goes, but it's solid at 8.  The integration of the theme works really well and the multiple paths are certainly there.  Only played with 3 so I'm curious to see what happens when there are more people shooting for the same limited resources.

The things that may keep the rating down:  
Brutal majority control scoring.  There is first and there is second and everyone else has wasted their investment.
Random Discovery strength requirement.  Getting hit with too many natives sucks - tons of investment lost.  Almost as bad is overcompensating.  You get a significantly smaller reward for weak discoveries compared to strong ones, but you have no idea which one you'll find until you get there.
----update----
A 7 is probably too generous.  But a 6 doesn't feel like it's necessarily enough.  As I don't give half points in my ratings this one will probably bounce around a bit until I figure out where it should settle.

---update---
I think it'll settle at 6.  There is some fun to be had here, but you can tell when you've been knocked out of contention half way through, and the game is too long to want to bear that too many times.  There are some interesting choices, but there are several forced choices and clearly sub-optimal choices as well.  The latter is masked by the action-denial mechanic, but I still feel as if interesting gameplay were sacrificed a few times too many for purposes of "theme" - particularly considering how thin that theme is overall.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Age Of Empires III has Worker Placement, Area Influence, and Set Collection, making it a solid Euro, but also has (limited) warfare which gives it a little something extra.

Its not an overly long game, but there can be some downtime while players choose where to place their colonists.

The components are a mixed bag. The board is terrible. There are no places on it for the trade goods and capital building components, even though there is wide-open space in the center of the board. The victory track is the worst I've seen; it is dark, narrow, and the corners are obscured by some kind of blob-like decoration. (I should note that the board in the Eagle-Gryphon reprint, while more functional, is so much uglier that I prefer the original.) The coins, on the other hand, are excellent reproductions of the currency of the era. The figurines are well-modeled and brightly colored, but some of them are hard to distinguish from others. 

On a personal note, although the game gives a pretty good representation of how the European powers viewed the colonization of the New World, I find the theme offensive. The goal of the game is to wipe out 'Indians' and extract trade goods from newly discovered areas; it feels like you're raping the New World. Ick.

AoEIII would get more plays, but a close friend despises it.

RATING: 6
Worker placement + area control. The base idea is an area control mechanic where you play items every turn, for 8 turns, with 3 scoring turns. You can deviate resources and build some economy and have several options that allow several (not many) viable strategies.

Great bits (a small complaint: it's a bit difficult to distinguish some of the specialists). Even overproduced. 

The base idea is very nice, themathic, and creates a tense atmosphere while placing the settlers.

The main issue with this game is that it's not really balanced, especially with the tiles. Some buildings are way better than others, to the point of being game changers. If you happen to be the start player when they become available for sale, you will be in a perfect place to win the game.

規則:
*每人起始10元，五個工人，多放一個在順位區，每後一個順位補償1元
*圖版除了加勒比海外每區隨機放一個蓋著的發現版塊跟一個對應貨物版塊，建築物按時代分類，隨機抽5個放在建築區，隨機抽4個貨物版塊放在交易區，放一艘船在商船區，殖民港格放一艘船標記可用格數(人數*2-1)，一艘船記回合
*遊戲總共進行8輪，每輪有固定階段
A.工人放置 照順位輪流放一個工人到對應位置(有格的從左開始放，不然就隨意放，專業人士格每格只能有一個人踩)，工人一定要放完
B.結算 有上到下左到右結算所有行動，所有使用完工人回到個人工人供應區(探索格不清)
>順位格按照放的位置拿對應錢，要到I階段才改變順位
>殖民港 按順字移動到已探索區，該區如果一有三個可拿走貨品版塊，專業人士可換回工人
>交易 照順位拿貨品版塊
>商船 人最多的拿走船
>建築 左到右按順序買建築物(10/14/20)元，建築物馬上有效，放了可不買
>探索 按順位一次那多個並決定要翻哪個探索版塊(全部人看)，人數大於等於該版塊獲得金錢(士兵額外的錢)並放一個工人在該區該版塊最後記分，其他回供應區，失敗蓋著放回去，工人仍要棄掉，全部地圖探索完可探索卡片
>專業人士 獲得對應專業人士，採訓練格的可以花5元訓練任一種，每次只能獲得一隻
>戰爭 可宣告單一或全圖戰爭，單一選擇一個區域跟一個對手，全圖付10元選擇一個對手，只要同區域有士兵跟對手工人就參戰，戰鬥結算，每隻士兵可以消滅一個對手單位，同時結算
C.收入 根據貨物版塊拿錢  三個不同1元，三個相同3元，四個相同6元，商船是百搭，但是一組只能有一艘   
D.建築好處 
E.補充建築版塊到5塊開著的，換時代時全部棄掉開新的5個
F.剩下的丟回供應區在補充新的4個貨物版塊
G.如果空了補充新的商船到商船區
H.拿5個基本工人
I.改變順位有放的根據放的位置移動，沒放的保持相對位置後移
J.移動回合標記一格
*專業人士功能 在殖民地的專業人是可以換成工人(士兵除外)
>船長 商船區跟發現行動格算兩個工人
>商人 商船區算兩個人/到殖民地時獲得5元
>傳教士 到殖民地時該地額外獲得一個工人
>士兵 探索成功時獲得探索版塊上對應的金錢(每個士兵算一次)/放殖民地時可進行戰爭
*順位決定每回合起始放工人順序，商船區平手時打破平手，探索時按順位開始
*每個時代結束會記分，每個區域有3個同色工人以上會記分，照數目拿6-2分，平手降一格拿分(3人平手都沒分)
*遊戲最後在算探索板塊卡片分數，殖民區域分同上，建築分，獲得同等於商品收入的分數，平首先比最後一次殖民分在比錢，在比貨物

RATING: 7
A mesh of mechanics that's never entirely won me over. Worker placement, tile drafts, area majorities. There's a constrant threat of non-Euro type mano-on-mano conflict breaking out at any time with the warfare option. You can be collateral damage if majority wars break out in your areas leaving other areas to score the same without nearly as much effort. There's also big resolution luck in what you get with your discoveries, which is a blind commitment of resource, for which you may way over subscribe and get a poor return (money, vps) or you play the odds committing just the resources which would land you 90% of the discoveries but get one of the high ones and miss out altogether, your resources effectively flushed down the drain. On things such does your end-result turn. It's certainly decent to play, and engaging, but the post-game analysis for the winner seems to be how things went luckily for them, and for the losers seems to be on the what-ifs re things outside their control, no matter how decent a strategy or its implementation. Happy to play occasionally, but no need to buy.

RATING: 8.5
Really good game with simple yet interesting worker placement mechanic. Not many games do that "different workers" stuff... The game is 7 years old now and plays really well compared to other, younger games. You have 5 different workers available but first you have to train them... You have to get goods to get more money, get ships to boost your goods collection... you need money to buy buildings that boost your workers numbers, VP and money... there's so much going on, it's a tight game. I love the fact that there are many possibilites and thus many strategies to come up with. You cannot focus on one action, you have to vary them, cause otherwise you will loose.
Great game that I got recently for a really good price... it's out of print but it will be back in print with new art and updated rules, that's what I heard at least.

RATING: 8
4-5 Players

*****House rules*****

**Scouting**: At any time during your turn, you may pay to look at any face-down Discovery counter or card. The cost is $1 in Age I, $2 in Age II, and $3 in Age III. After looking at a face-down counter, return it to the same position, facedown.

OR

**Advanced Reconnaissance**: Before the game begins, turn all Discovery counters on the board face up. This will eliminate the possibility of failed expeditions and
increase the need for strategic planning.

**Buy Victory Points**:
players may (in Player Order) spend Spanish Dollars to buy Victory Points immediately following New World colony scoring. Players may buy as many Victory Points as they can afford. The cost is:
• Age I: $1 / 1 VP
• Age II: $3 / 1 VP
• Age III: $10 / 1 VP

**Reduced Luck Variant** — During setup, draw 10 of the Age III Buildings and discard the rest. Leave these Buildings face-up until Age III. These 10 are the potential Buildings that will come out in Age III. Randomize these 10 Buildings before refreshing the game board for turn 7.

**Buildings**

The $5 colonist spot can be used for $5 but also a 'building materials' can be purchased here - ie there are now two spots on this space, not one.

A building materials can be used either to lower the cost of
A Capital Building purchase by $5 or can be transported like a colonist to a territory where it increases the value of that territory +4 VP for first +2 VP for second.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
UPDATE: I have played this more than 10 times now, and it's only getting better. Can't wait to play more.

***

There is one thing I truley dislike in one of my 10:s. It is that generous board that actually proves to be insufficient. The small land-zones gets to crowded during a five-player game, and that big logotype above Canada takes up space that might have been precious landmass. I'm sure there is a perfectly good corporate reason for the size of the logo, too bad it also looks so tacky. With a board big enough to at least fill up the box, it would have been more practical even with the current map design.

(The small scoring track works just fine using the useless fountain markers from the dull Alhambra game; blue, green, yellow, red and orange - a perfect fit for AOEIII.)

But enough with my endless whining, AOEIII proves that the sum can be better than it's parts. It is an excellent game that gets better with every play. It is exiting, competitive and with 5 players it is always fierce (4 is almost as good & 3 works pretty fine). Play with fun loving people and a couple of beers and you won't go wrong.

It's not hard on non/casual gamers, but the lush production/detailed components might contrast to peoples expectations. At first glance AOEIII looks like something much more "hands on", but the merchant ships are "only" jokers and there is no strong sense of battle when your soldiers fight. The miniatures are pawns to use in chess like placement mechanics that gives a slight abstract feel to the game. AOEIII really IS competitive and fun, but at first sight you just might expect another kind of game.

Once you start playing it, it's not really that complicated although you are forced to make agonizing decisions all the time. But for some reason I find it much harder to explain the rules of AOEIII than, say Puerto Rico, Imperial or Power Grid. I think it due to all the specialist figures, and how they perfom when putting them in different places. Your strategy takes shape or alters ever so slightly with every single placement.

I was more reminded somewhat of Struggle of Empires. Struggle is more complex, aggressive and feels more concrete, but I prefer the sleeker, quicker and more stringent AOEIII.  

I play with discoveries tiles open.

RATING: 6
It's probably the best of Glenn Drover's efforts and a nice overall design. 

It's not innovative, but it's good. It's yet another worker placement game with unique buildings thrown in a a bit of exploration. Randomness is present but is manageable and makes sense, most of the time (with the exception being in the available buildings). 

Game length is on the upper limit but it's not an impediment.  

I have some issues with the components (unnecessarily huge box, badly designed board and I don't really like plastic miniatures) but that's what I've learned to expect from eagle's productions.

Overall a solid game.

RATING: 7
1/11/12 - I traded Memoir 44 for this game a long time ago and I might be regretting my decision. At first this game was awesome. There were tons of components and it seemed like a blast to play. Now that I've played better worker placement games the components seem way too similar which really bothers me and the units seem to be jam packed on the board so it becomes hard to tell what units are what. The game play also seems a lot more boring to me now. Unfortunately, when I traded for this game the box was damaged so I had to repair it. If the box was nice I totally would get rid of this in a heartbeat.

2/01/12 - I played this again and it was actually really fun. The similar looking figure models still drive me crazy but I like how the game has a set number of turns and it was much more enjoyable when I was in a better mood. I guess I'll be keeping this game.

7/15/12 - I still hate how the figure models look so similar. I also hate how lame the choices get toward the end of a 5 player game. I really can't get over how similar the figure models are and how fiddly the game is. I will try it again but I've downgraded it from a 7 to a 6.

RATING: 7
I've only played this once so far, but was very impressed with it.  Importantly the game just made sense while reading the rules.  Certain rules mechanics are borrowed from, or inspired by, other games and thus have a degree of familiarity which helps get to grips with it; the imported/inspired bits have been very well chosen however and work very well together.  The initiative/extra money track is a good innovation and has a simple but effective "swings and roundabouts" effect.  
On one playing only it's hard to comment on strategy; at this stage it looks like there are plenty of viable routes to win while none seem to be the obvious "easy way" to win.  

The board is lovely; a good balance between decent artwork and well-laid out game mechanics.  The figures are good (and if painted up will probably look fantastic) and even the plastic money (styled on doubloons) is great.

RATING: 7
A very interesting game that, as with many Kramer games, sets down the basic rules and allows the game to develop in a variety of ways. I find the play more enjoyable than Caylus as it feels more fluid and less forced. It is nice to see a Euro-style game with the plastic minis rather than the typical wooden blocks as it not only adds flavor to the game but speeds up the analysis of the game. (A captain, for example, is a figure with a spyglass and is quickly recognized versus being a slightly larger block, etc.) The game scales well and it is impressive how well the various systems are integrated.  The "War" aspect is minimal if nearly non-existent as there are so many other places to play. However, a well placed soldier/battle can change the majority in an area and conceivably, the winner. (Ignore soldiers are your own  risk.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Liked it very much - lots of different strategies and the game ended very close.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
-Limited Availability
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Worker Placement and area control fused into a delicious sandwich. The way the action board resolves is very similar to, but less harsh than, Dominant Species. One of the interesting things here is you can use a few different spots to turn your workers into specialty workers who will count for twice the strength or unlock a special ability when used. The game starts out where everything seems possible, and then you realize as the colonist dock fills up, and as people grab the trade goods, and as the player ahead of you in turn order drops his captain in the merchant ship box, that you'll have to do better to get what you want.

The board is very pretty, but needs a major redesign for usability. This is a seriously, almost to the point of non-functional board. The new world provinces are too tiny and all the plastic workers have to be bunched up to prevent clarity as to where they stand. I suppose they had to make them small so they could print "AGE OF EMPIRES III" in huge letters at the top of the board. Even on the actions board the graphic design of "PUT A WORKER HERE" is inconsistent. If there's ever a second edition, I'm there.

This game was renamed because they lost the license to Age of Empires III. They neglected to ever print a copy of the game with the new name... so yeah... confusion. Also, the expansion comes with new AND replacement buildings, because the backs of the tiles were printed differently.

RATING: 8
[b]RO[/b]: [i]07/05/2015[/i]

One of the first games I ever acquired in the hobby and consistently one of the highest rated. Age of Empires III (also known more recently as Glenn Drover's Empires, presumably due to some copyright law issues) is an interesting and thematically rich euro. It has aspects of set collection, economic balance, light warfare, and competition for limited action spaces. It is tight, tense, and interesting and I believe falls somewhere in the medium range of weight. The components are lovely, especially the coins which I found myself re-purposing for other games. A solid title to be sure.

RATING: 7
My only play of this was with six players.  Six players are too many!!  But here are my thoughts regarding a six player game.

I like the different action spots.  There are a lot of different choices and the players must decide not only where to put their pieces but [i]when[/i] to put their pieces.

There area control aspect is also very nifty.  The unusual combination of area control and worker placement is refreshing.

There's no way to combat a run-away leader.  If everyone tries to beat up on a run-away leader, they will neglect their own business.  In my only play, it was obvious who was going to win about an hour and a half into the game.  It took us four hours (only three of that was actual game time)!  Half of the game was spent already knowing who would win.  That's just too much for me.

Can't believe I waited 3 years to play this. I suppose my track record with Eagle Games is to blame.

(12/27/07) - [8] - With 2 games under my belt I am definitely a fan. Multiple paths to victory and excellently balanced. Lots of fun with 2, would be absolutely amazing with more! Currently rated at an 8.

(2/22/08) - [7] - After playing my first 3-player game today I'm worried about the balance of the game. There appears to many paths to victory, but the surefire way to win seems to be whoever can get the most Merchant Ships. Re-rated to a 7.

(10/11/08) - [3] - Turns out that in previous games we had been incorrectly implementing Merchant Ships, making them extremely overpowered. Even after fixing this error, I have realized this game is broken. A player who is devoted to discovering can hope to earn 6VPs every scoring round for a total of 18VPs. A player who simply piles up Merchant Ships and Trade Goods can get either the Navy or Mercantile Building in Age 3 and earn 20+ VPs. I don't expect to ever play this again.

(09/21/2014) - [2] - It only took me six years, but I finally looked closer at the BGG rating guide and determined that Age of Empires III qualifies as a [2].

(11/02/2016) - SOLD - Age of Empires III made the final trip to Goodwill today. I did not ever foresee a time that I would want to play this again. A long overdue removal from my collection.

RATING: 8.2
Excellent Euro-style game with American style aesthetic. There are several viable strategies for winning and it scales well-  though each additional player adds more confrontation to the game and gives the game a different tone. 

Luck is relatively minor, but the random elements work to keep the game fresh and allow it to play differently every game. Replay value has been very good, mostly due to the random elements that vary from game to game.

AoE III is not a short game, but it plays fast. Some games are long games and  feel  like long games. This is more in the Die Macher style- in that the game goes by at a good pace and you don't realize it just took 2 hours to play. There is very little downtime and you are always involved.

RATING: 8
I really like all the different paths to victory. There's a lot of tension with such tight competition for limited resources. I'm really impressed with how the mechanics were implemented in this game. While a player can spend a lot of time analyzing to situation to make the optimum move, the chaos factor associated with the number options available, the dynamic turn order, and the number of players keep the outcome ever in doubt as you can never be sure how your planned course of action will turn out. There is often times no "absolute" best move in this game, and I really like that in a game. As a 4-5 player game, I'd rate this a 9 or 10. As a 2-3 player game, I'd give it a 7 or 8. The pace of the game and the competition for resources is quite different depending on the number of players. 2-3 is fun 4-5 is much better.

RATING: 6
--May2007-- Fascinating game with (in my opinion) some graphic faults.  I'd personally find counters or blobs of wood in different shapes easier to parse visually than the Captain/Soldier figures (or Merchant/Settler to a slightly lesser extent) which blend together into a homogeneous blob from across the table.  Equally, by the end of the game the new world is _full_ of plastic; smaller objects (or stackable objects) would tile better and be easier to evaluate.  Once one's past the visuals the game seems very good (modulo one's tolerance of the random discovery results, at least.)
--Jun2007-- Like Caylus, it's a bit long for what it is.  Too much time is spent on the mechanics of playing the game, and not enough on the actual playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
[color=#CC0000]

The epitome of the Power Euro.  This game has one of everything in it, and to some extent it succeeds at that task.  But I'll leave it to you to decide if that's a good thing or not.

I'm coming to fully realize how much I dislike worker placement and this game is a big part of what convinced me.  It's another game where you don't get to do what you want to, you get to do what's left, regardless of whether it's useful to you or not.  The nature of the mechanic is to dis-empower the player.  It's competition, but it's not play.  A two-hour session of "what's left" becomes two more hours of work in my congested schedule, and I don't get paid to do it.

For what it's worth.

If you like this game you'll very likely enjoy [b][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/9625]Struggle of Empires[/url][/b] more.  Much more.  It's an order of magnitude more intense and engaging, and provides pathways for you to make [u]your[/u] game work, instead of playing "what's left."

[/color]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
This is a clever civilization simulation where players take on the task of discovering and settling The New World. Each turn players get a set of figures, each one granting an ability, and they then place them on the board indicating what each will do.  Actions vary from colonizing, acquiring goods, constructing buildings, discovering new territories, upgrading a figure, and even battling.  Many actions grant points and/or money; at the end of the game the player with the most points wins. 

Players never have enough figures to do everything they want in a turn, and given there are only a few slots available per action players are competing for these. Each turn players must carefully weigh what they want and what the other players will likely want. There are diverse strategies, and no two games are the same.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A good, if a bit uninspired, medium worker placement, a step up from Stone Age but firmly below Agricola. Some luck, some negative interaction, ability to develop varying strategies, scales as well from 2 to 6 (if you get another set of colonist, for example from the expansion) as anything else. Lacks the intensity and tightness of heavier games, but it only makes it more accessible to casual gamers. Recommended.

RATING: 8
So much better and faster with three players than with five - I've changed my rating to reflect my opinion on this as a three and maybe even the four (experienced) player game. Engaging and fun - can drag a little in places as players become familiar with building abilities. A balanced spread of points across the different methods of earning them seems like a good bet. Discovery luck irks me a bit - maybe playing face up I'll enjoy it more - thematically though I like face down better. Box is flimsy and damaged on arrival. You really need to paint the specialists in some way so they can be differentiated by other players at a glance (not just those on the board, but also those waiting to be played off the board) - it's very annoying thinking you've got the merchant sown up only to realise you've mistaken an off board merchant for a settler. The design of the board could have done with more room in the colonies, all the specialists special abilities outlines should be shown, including when placed as colonists. The building tile abilities could have been differentiated for easy viewing from the other side of the table. Rules are vague in places (I was wondering what I did with the remaining eight discovery tiles), and scattered - the rulebook starts off in great detail and then becomes vague to the point where I need to search for the FAQ online. Faced with playing Caylus or this I think this might get more play because of the theme, but in so many ways it doesn't deserve to. fun for now while I continue to explore the strategies.

RATING: 9
A brilliant mixture between [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/18602]Caylus[/i][/url], [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/24480]Pillars of the Earth[/i][/url], [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/93-El%20Grande%20Decennial]El Grande[/i][/url] (majority games) and [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/3076]Puerto Rico[/i][/url] that takes known mechanics to develop a very exciting and easy to learn game. Aztan elkezdtem olvasni a beszamolokat a jatekrol es egy kicsit megijedtem, mert nem tunt annak a kalandozos, felfedezos, fejlodos jateknak, mint az otletet ado PC-s valtozat. RATING: 6

I like that there are different types of workers, with certain workers being better in certain spaces.

RATING: 7

Edit 01-12-08 I piped it down a bit from a 9 to an 8. Don't know why exactly but I felt after my last game (which I won) like I just didn't care anymore... It happened on more then one occasion that I or others had to ask whose turn it is. As another poster here said too, that is typically a sign that players are not really involved / experience angst or anything like that. Hope this feeling will not stick...

Update: I don't know... there is something about the game that makes me want to play less and less. I can't say I look forward to playing it, and then again I don't find it a crime either when I do play. I suppose I never get fully immerged by AoeIII 'anymore' or... was I ever? I played Caylus the other day, and although it has nothing to do with that game apart from the worker placement, that kind of 'angst' never comes to me in a game of AoeIII. The stress of 'my spot being taken by another player' is seldom felt. Some of that has to do with the fact that the only truly place to feel this 'angst' is in the Capitol Building placement, since it is the most comparable perhaps to a single worker-building. But if taken by someone else earlier in turn order, that feels like a done deal and there is nothing that you really could have done about it (besides being earlier in turn order, duh). So it's easier to just sigh and be done with it. Apart from that the game bogs down much faster to 'scripted' placing and many times players seem to forget it's their turn. Finally I'm beginning to relate to the people that are sometimes surprised by the fact that they have done so well/bad in the game, when it's time to score. Sometimes someone wins or loses and I have no clue why that happened. I have an idea, but what it is exactly... That can be frustrating as it is a sign that I don't really understand what is going on in the game. Which is strange jet again because I've played it almost two dozen times...

RATING: 8.1
[COLOR=#663300][b][size=11]Age of Empires III: The Age of Discovery[/size][/b][/COLOR]
[COLOR=#663300][i]Note: My numeric ratings disregard BGG's associated verbal descriptions.[/i][/COLOR]

[COLOR=#006600]Version played: [/COLOR]2007 Tropical Games, 1st Edition
[COLOR=#006600]Version owned: [/COLOR]2008 Tropical Games, 2nd Edition
[COLOR=#006600]Play History: [/COLOR]
 [COLOR=#006600] • 2 players: [/COLOR] -
 [COLOR=#006600] • 3 players: [/COLOR] Twice.  Don't bother at three.  Mechanics flourish with more players.
 [COLOR=#006600] • 4 players: [/COLOR] Several times
 [COLOR=#006600] • 5 players: [/COLOR] -

[COLOR=#006600]Instructions: [/COLOR]The rulebook is long and thorough.  It includes pictorial examples and useful indexes, and the back cover shows a turn summary and a list of possible victory points. 
[COLOR=#006600]Learning Curve: [/COLOR] While reading the rulebook will necessarily take time, teaching the game will not.  The learning curve is rather quick.
[COLOR=#006600]Depth: [/COLOR] Medium — A fan of medium-weight euro games would likely find this satisfying.
[COLOR=#006600]Luck: [/COLOR] Some — Capital Buildings, Trade Goods, and Discoveries.
[COLOR=#006600]Downtime: [/COLOR]Not much.  The turn phases are streamlined.
[COLOR=#006600]Fiddliness: [/COLOR]Alas, set-up is fiddly.
[COLOR=#006600]Components: [/COLOR]Top-notch, both in terms of the detailed plastic bits, and the rich artwork. Fans of Ameritrash should be pleased.
[COLOR=#006600]Player Interaction: [/COLOR]High
[COLOR=#006600]Conflict: [/COLOR]Potential increases with more players.
[COLOR=#006600]Theme: [/COLOR]Both the components and the mechanics contribute to a highly thematic experience.

[COLOR=#006600]Travel related: [/COLOR] [COLOR=#CC0000]Unsuitable.[/COLOR]
[COLOR=#006600]• Size: [/COLOR]Large
[COLOR=#006600]• Weight: [/COLOR]Heavy
[COLOR=#006600]• Table Required: [/COLOR]Yes
[COLOR=#006600]• Footprint: [/COLOR]Large
[COLOR=#006600]• Component Trackability: [/COLOR]Poor
[COLOR=#006600]• Replaceability: [/COLOR] Available as of Jan 2010

[COLOR=#006600][b]Comments: [/b][/COLOR]

The theme is well integrated into the mechanics.  For example, discovery must take place before colonization; and missionaries convert natives.

Simply one of the best worker-placement games I've played.  It takes a while to play, and it's high in both strategy and randomness.  In fact, I think it finds a good balance between the two.  

[COLOR=#006600][b]Why we own it: [/b][/COLOR] Unlike other worker placement games, in addition to the generic workers, some worker tokens have specializations — making them better suited to some tasks than others.
