RATING: 7.5
Rather enjoyable euromesh-of-mechanics boardgame. Think Caylus (you place colonists to take actions, you upgrade units and buy buildings for extra powers) and Goa (you attempt discovery missions) and El Grande (you dump colonists into newly discovered land for area control VPs). Essentially, players are trying to gain VPs from a variety of methods including area discovery, set collection, area majorities, building purchases. There's a nice feel of escalating tension as the game goes through the ages.

The plumage on this one is really something (beautiful board, plastic bits, coins etc). Though it feel rather 'been there, done that', this one actually plays well, is balanced and much less laboured than Caylus.

Igazi ereje a játéknak a többféle stratégia és itt a többféle tényleg nagyon sokat jelent. Egy játék alapján is látszott, hogy nagyon sok út vezethet a győzelemhez. Fontos része a játéknak a taktika is. Figyelni a többi játékos tervét és ahhoz alkalmazkodni, egy körön belül is megváltoztatni az eredeti célkitűzést és máshova rakni a telepeseket. Nagyon komoly játék, amiben felfedeznek, hódítanak, építenek, terményeket gyűjtenek és harcolnak is a játékosok. Egyedül talán a játékidő lehet sokak számára hosszú, de úgy érzem így teljes a játék. RATING: 5
After my first overly long play of AoE III everyone immediately wanted to try to fix it (including those that had played before).  Even after years of trying to get this game right it seems that they reached the deadline and decided that it was now or never.  I suspected as much immediately when those that played it before exclaimed that the money doesn't make any sense.  The gold should be fives instead of tens otherwise you run out of ones -- most things are payed out less than ten.  Not a huge gripe by any means, but an indication of potential problems.  It was immediately apparent that the new world map was going to be confusing once pieces started getting scrunched in. So perhaps with obvious development flaws in front of us, I was  too keenly suspicious.  Still, I had heard good things about this from people that I respect, so I wanted to give it a real go.

The brunt of luck bears a serious burden on the design.  More than just the absurdity of the exploration rewards/penalties (which should not be discounted), the "Building" tiles and the Trade Goods draw are just too random to plan for.  In the game you can play for initiative, but you don't actually know what you are trying to get in first for.  Like many drafting games, those that are in the right place at the right time have the advantage.  There is some minor decision making in your selection, but often the choices are very clear.  Once you have set down a certain path with trade goods, you will need to get those particular trade goods, if they don't come up you are humped, if they do you need to hope that no one else takes them from you.  The fact that they are trade goods, yet you cannot trade them with people seems absurd.

The difference of a few coins in the first turn are a pittance to the disadvantage of a poor draw in turn order.  Not only do you not have the advantage of getting worthwhile buildings, but you also need to spend a guy to get to a relevant position in the next turn.

Players that have more pieces have the advantage (which is fine, I guess), but when they have residual pieces they can just gain money after people drop out with the turn order, which makes lagging players lag a little farther behind as they are farther behind in turn order and farther behind with money.

My biggest complaint is that at the beginning of every turn you must do the math to figure out if other people are going to be able to beat you to certain things, the race for the merchant ships is especially tiring.  The pieces are difficult to recognize anyway, trying to asses whether it will be worth it to try to gain dominance means that you actually have to spend time asking each player if they can beat you.  If not you effectively lose your turn.  You must do this kind of analysis when placing you pieces into each region as well.  

I find this game systematic, completely reactionary yet without any real excitement. Everything here feels cobbled together. People compare this game to Caylus or Puerto Rico, but for me everything that this game tries to do, Leonardo DaVinci does more elegantly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It just has everything a Euro should have. The rules are eloquent and easy to explain, the bits are amazing, the pacing of game play is perfect, just the right amount (very little) of luck, player interaction of the space-taking, not so nasty sort, etc. The warring is such a small part of this game that confrontation-haters will still feel comfortable here. And it's just a joyful experience to play. 

One thing I truly respect in the design and absolutely love about the gameply is that there is really no "upkeep" mechanism here to have to deal with. You never have the hassle and stress of having to feed your family like in Agricola or deal with polution like in Antiquity, or unhappiness and hunger like in Through the Ages. Makes for a less frustrating, but still tight, strategic and fun game - brilliant, really. Almost makes you wonder why some games have such upkeep mechanics and brutal punishments within their design. 

The drawbacks I find all have to do with the board, which really, really should have been redesigned before production: the victory track is a joke, as it is too small and just plain silly. Also, the regions are too small for when more and more figures start pilin' up. Oh-one more thing, i'm not too impressed with the art on the tiles, as they are thematic, but generally hard to read from a distance.

RATING: 8
A fun game, but seems like a pretty typical euro in the line of Leonardo da Vinci and Caylus: allocate workers to different areas, then resolve the areas to get the appropriate benefits. This game, however, is much more interesting than either of those: there is more going on but it's not overly complicated and it plays relatively quickly (with 4). The randomness of the order capitol buildings show up is a potential problem -- turn order (which you can fiddle with but which only takes effect the NEXT turn) may not allow you to purchase a useful one. My biggest quibble, though, is the production: the box is ginormous, has a LOT of empty space, and is a nonstandard size that won't stack well with any of our other games! Also, some of the colonists (peons and merchants) are a little hard to distinguish from each other quickly, and they don't fit on the score track -- a FEW wooden cubes/discs would have been nice!

RATING: 7
Has the feel of a Eurogame, which is good and bad here.  The good is there are many interesting choices each round with your resource allocations.  On the other hand, I prefer a little more randomness in conflict resolution.  Here it feels a bit too mechanical for a game where you're discovering, conquering, and developing new lands. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game is a mixture between worker placement and area control.  Each turn players place their workers on a variety of different spaces that discover and populate parts of the "New World", collect trade goods, specialist workers, and battle opponents.

It can be compared to Dominant Species where worker placement is also used to manage the area control mechanics of a game.  I didn't really care for that game and I don't like this one much better.  I do like it a little better because it doesn't seem as abstract as Dominant Species.  The one slightly original mechanic of this game are the specialist workers that provide bonuses based on what action spaces they are placed in.

I think I just need to stop playing these older games so long after their release because I tend not to like them with a few exceptions.  Worker placement has been refined so much and knocked out of the park by so many games since this came out that this game just seems kind of bland and samey even if it was original at the time it was released.  Even the mechanic I labeled as "slightly original" where you can acquire different kinds of workers with special abilities has been done and done better by several other games since.

All in all this game was simply bland.  The worker placement piece, which with how much I like worker placement should have been my favorite piece, just seemed ancillary and unnecessary to the area control piece.  The special buildings you could buy didn't do anything very interesting and all of this made the game kind of bland.  The worst was that there was very little to do by the end game, especially the final turn.

It wasn't all bad though.  There are multiple legitimate paths to victory and lots of interesting decisions to make.  It's just not something that has stood the test of time.

RATING: 8
A great strategy game where every game is always different. There are many things to do to achieve ultimate victory: trade, exploration of new territories, building of pre-and battles. It looks complex, but the clarity of the regulation makes it very easy to learn and play. The things to do are well defined in each round of the players and the strategies adopted must always be different every game to obstruct opponents. Good materials and weave game, while the board could be slightly larger to allow better placement of the checkers game. The miniatures are not the greatest perfection, and often confuse those of the merchant and settler. Overall you are having fun and the length of matches is never long and frustrating. It's perfect for 4 players, while for 3 players is a bit more limited because you do not have time to discover all the territories, but have fun anyway. Highly recommended!

RATING: 7
I've played this game 3 times now with anywhere from 3 to 5 opponents.  I was initially unimpressed with the fact that it bore absolutely no resemblance to the AoE and AoE II computer games that I got addicted to in the past.  Then I realized what a really well-designed and beautifully-produced (other than the score track and the map regions) game it truly is, with many interesting choices that must be made each turn, a high level of competition between players,  multiple paths to victory, and the fact that it comes close to leaving me with a similar "narrative" feeling that I get after playing a large and long, conquer-the-world-style AT game.  (I'm sure this is partly due to the fact that it is designed by Glenn Drover, and partly due to the fact that it has a ton of plastic, including truly astonishing ships that bring to mind the "empty city" markers in Railroad Tycoon - ya gotta love Eagle Games.)  And THEN I realized that there is a certain element of excitement that is missing, and that, while it is still better than most large, Euro-style game I've played, I would have a lot more fun playing Age of Mythology.  So did they succeed in bringing the AoE computer games to 3-D life?  No.  Did they succeed in taking a tricky theme that incorporates many elements and turning it into a fairly fun, relatively simple yet challenging gaming experience?  Yes!  Game design: 4.5/10  Personal enjoyment: 2.5/10  Total: 7/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is easily Glenn Drover's best game; which isn't saying very much.
Actually, this is a surprisingly good euro-style game.  The theme is strong and the gameplay is pretty well refined.
Strangely enough, my largest complaint is with the production.  In typical Eagle Games (well in this case Tropical Games) style, the board is huge and lavishly decorated and the pieces are elaborate molded plastic figures (which, thankfully, we don't have to pry off of sprues this time).  They look fantastic.  Unfortunately they aren't very functional.  The different pieces are too difficult to distinguish from one another at a glance, they're constantly falling over, and the victory track around the board is almost unusable.  Victory tracks should be very clear and the pieces would have been much easier to use if they were simple wooden shapes (such as the ones used in Game of Thrones for instance).
My biggest gameplay complaint is that the discovery cards introduce just a bit too much luck in a game that otherwise is very strategic.
Neither of these complaints should be enough to deter you from trying what otherwise is a fine game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A good game, with mechanisms slightly similar to Caylus or Maestro Leonardo, but it avoids the bean-counter feeling I get with those two games, perhaps through having a richer theme, perhaps by having what feels like a greater variety in its ways of earning victory points. Played with 3, 4, 5 and 6 players, and enjoyed every game.

Now, having said that, there are issues. I'd rather use pencil & paper than the scoretrack, the New World colonies are too small, it's easy to make mistake in identifying figures, it's normally too expensive/counter-productive to take a trade-good or capital building just to stop someone else getting it, first-turn capital buildings can be a gift for the player randomly drawn to go first (I'm looking at you, Mr "take $20"), competing for merchant ships normally revolves around looking to see how many pieces a player has left to place, and I haven't yet seen anyone win whose main strategy was scoring in the colonies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

A very good euro-game with an essential random element and nice figures instead of cubes. I like AoEIII because of its theme (conquering the New World) that you can strongly feel during playing. The mechanics is decent, there are some distinct paths to the victory. There is a significant interaction between players, your opponents can press you very strongly, but they cannot smash you. To win the game, you need to observe your enemies and not be stuck to a single tactic. Sometimes, we use AoE to introduce new people into the boardgame world and the opinions are mostly very positive. Personally, I'm always playing with a pleasure.

The main game drawback: the box is really huge, it's difficult to transport it to my friends. It could be twice smaller, at least.

Number of plays: about 17.
Last games: LW

RATING: 7
Over time, this game has fallen by the wayside.  The Area Control is much to fixed to be interesting as once colonists are sent to the new world, not much changes.

The Worker Placement over and over seem to be driven by turn order, in that most of the time, people are going after the same things each round, and only their placement in turn order is what keeps it.  

It is better with the bid for starting player order to take away the rush for the turn 1 capital buildings, and some of the capital buildings are just plain overpowered while others end up being quite weak.

It's also not too quick a game either, and for just a little longer, you can play Dominant Species, which has rendered Age of Empires III obsolete in my opinion.

RATING: 7
Worker placement and area control. You can send your worker on the initiative track (the next players will earn hear more money), on the dock (he goes on the main board), take goods (gives you money),to the ship (joker good), buy a building (helps you in the game and some bring points), make a discovery (points and money), take a specialist (4 different, better than standard colonist) and declare agression/war.

Buildings are important, but the`re expensive (you have not much money). The most important is the majority in the regions on the board and making discoveries (you can risk). 

Your colonist and specialists are miniatures. The problem is some of them looks similar, so you must ask the other players what person they have left or on the board. On the board there is not so much place in the regions. The game is long. Good game, but nothing special.

RATING: 8
Now reimplemented as the mouthful, [thing=173442][/thing], on first blush I found this game bland. I sarcastically thought, "Oh joy. Another worker placement game with a seafaring /settlement theme. How novel." Upon multiple plays, however, this game really started to grow on me. It shines with 3 or more players. You need to learn how all of the different strategies work together before you can play effectively. The warfare element really puts it over the top and adds a lot of tension because a player can subvert another player's pure building strategy.

Component-wise, this game has always been a bit overblown and outsized. The board is big, the pieces are plastic miniatures, and even the box is large. Too large. I just don't have the patience these days for enormous boxes. I want to be able to transport my games to game night for frequent use. With a large box, the likelihood of me playing a game more than occasionally goes way down. After spotting the box for the reimplementation, wow, it seems I'm in the minority because the new edition is bigger and heavier. Shame. It's a game worth playing, but it's not one I want to lug around.

RATING: 7
A euro with a great theme, aka modern boardgame design, with surprisingly strong interaction.

A lot of available rewarding strategies to follow, a lot of interaction (it's a worker placement with area control), great theme, good sense of accomplishment and tough decisions create a very tense and enjoyable game.

Although the area control element is very well implemented, it's still something I personally do not enjoy very much. War, even though it opens a lot of strategic options and boosts up interaction still opens up kingmaking. The expeditions, though excellent from a thematic point of view, create risk in an otherwise luck free and tight strategy game.

Overall Age of Discovery is a game that has stood surprisingly well the test of time. It's still a great game to play, and offers some of the best of both worlds (strategy and theme)

RATING: 9
Simply put this is the best of the Eagle Games line...oh wait this is by TROPICAL GAMES...that makes it different right? Whatever, now let's talk the game.  So my only real gripe is it seems to take longer to play it than I think it should...I'm not sure why.  We even did pretty good with keeping focused on who's turn it was.  I'm not saying this game drags so don't read it like that, it just takes a solid 2 hours to play this game and it seems like it should be quicker.  It's not boring and I know I'm making it seem like this is bad but I felt I needed to say it.  Now the game itself is awesome.  Full of very tough choices.  It's a beautiful system that feels good in this theme too.  Just the right amount of randomness in the random resource tiles, and buildings coming out each turn and then the random exploration tiles/cards.  Lots of paths to victory so game play style can be custom to each player.  Very fun game can't wait to play it again.

Addendum:
I had been thinking this game might creep into the coveted 10 spot but now I don't believe that will happen.  After several more games it seems the missionary may be a bit too powerful.  We've now seen this tactic take a run away lead multiple times. If this is the case in future plays I think I'll end up lowering my rating for the game. I find this a bit ironic because it seems to me that people (myself included) in their first few games gloss over the power of the missionary and focus on other specialists.  Read through these comments and you'll see what I mean.  The big focus in our group didn't come up until we'd been playing for several games.  There is dissension amongst us as to whether or not he's over powered but we have agreed to give it a few more run through's and see what pans out.

Scoring mainly comes from area majority. That means it's terrible with fewer than at least 4.

RATING: 9
This was one of the better worker placement games of the past couple years.  The worker placement genre is certainly a crowded field these days with every other gaming seemingly using that mechanism since Caylus was released 3 years ago.  However, AOE3 was interesting and engaging.  The components were definitely over the top, especially the merchant ships, and the plastic pieces were actually harder to distinguish than simpler components would have been, not to mention the enormous board which is very much unnecessarily large, but despite all that, I found myself enjoying this one and the interesting decisions that it presented.

Received as gift: Christmas 2008

I will be happy playing this one for many years.

RATING: 7
Age of Empires is a solid worker placement game that has a touch of War to make it less passive than a typical Euro.  There is nothing amazing here that makes it stand out from the crowded class of similar games in the current environment, except for the minis, especially the boats!  Having only played with four it seems like it would scale up better than down.  With any less players the game would lose some tension because very little of the worker placement options disappear to compensate for less workers to place.  On the other hand, turns move fast so there would be minimal down time even with six (if you include expansion).  As a somewhat more interactive competitive Euro it will hold a solid place in any collection that needs to facilitate a larger gaming group.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Really fun.  As a man who likes Caylus well enough, but loves AT, this game scratches a very particular itch quite well.  In fact, I can't see myself playing much Caylus outside of BSW, since this takes the core worker placement mechanism, gives you something more fun to do than build some guy's castle, and has oodles of plastic.

It plays fast, yet offers lots of room for decision-making.  People say that capital buildings are over-powered, but I think that they're just powerful, and since they're the only thing you can really spend money on other than a wildcard specialist, you need to incorporate the purchase of at least a few capital buildings into your plan, but I don't think that they're a dominant strategy.

RATING: 10
While the rulebook is vague on some points, and so are the tiles, and the scoring track around the board is the worst one ever in the history of gaming, the game itself is a very challenging area control affair.  The new iteration has some bells and whistles in terms of its storage insert and player boards, also having the Builder expansion worked in, but its retail price is just not commensurate with the scope of the game, so this earlier version is just fine with me.  I consider it to be fairly thematic, and while the structure of placement and resolution might seem odd at first, you get used to it.  I rated it at 10 because it takes El Grande and evolves upon its basic premise, creating not an entirely new game, but certainly a refinement upon El Grande's design and intent.

RATING: 8
This is a great game. I cannot believe that I never played it before now. It is like a cross between a majority and a worker placement game, but with all the player interactivity that I can handle. Sending soldiers to the colonies and reducing the number of your opponents' colonists by means of war has got to be a first in what is decidedly a euro game. There are just so many options where you can spend your workers on: Discoveries, trade goods, trade good jokers, building tiles, etc., etc. I also like the fact that the rules are pretty straightforward, so you can start playing after only a short explanation. Definitely a keeper!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A medium-to-heavy weight worker placement game that I feel, successfully blends different elements of the PC game into a board game version. Players put down colonists in different categories each round to nab roles to improve the expansion of an empire in the New World and beyond. Mainly, players can pursue different tracks to obtain victory points. I found that even though worker placement games can be rather abstract, there are clearly pathways to specialize your empire so you can accumulate wealth, accelerate discoveries of new territories, improve economic wealth by improving cities and buildings, expand territories either by conquest with armies or by spreading the gospel via missionaries. These subtle routes to victories really capture the taste and flavor of the game. A very well designed albeit slightly overproduced game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
First of all, I just plain don't like the action drafting mechanic, so this game wasn't made for me in the first place, but still, I can appreciate, if not love, games like Caylus or Puerto Rico where the mechanic is well-implemented and has a clever and interesting game built around it. AoE3 just seems poorly conceived. The Area Control is brutal, so much so that in a 5-player game it seems to be best to stay out of the colonies entirely, except to pick up trade goods, which themselves are a stupid part of the game. They're "trade" goods, but you can't trade them! Finally, that the randomness of the Discoveries is ridiculous goes without saying, so I won't mention it, but the randomness of which buildings and trade goods come up and when is also quite silly.

In sum, I think this game just proves that quote from the Good Book: "Render to the Germans the games that are German, and to the Americans the games that are American." This is a Euro without all the mechanical interaction and fine-tuning that make the best Euros great, and as an American game, it's as dull as dirt, as it severely lacks in drama.

Oh and also, way too long. And I haven't even addressed the physical design.

RATING: 9
I very much enjoy this game.  One of the few games that had a lot of hype surrounding its release that has lived up to the hype.  There's enough going on that you always want to do twice as much as you're allowed to do.  Need to balance income, exploration, development, and warfare, all while making sure that you're still on good footing for the future.  Everything demands your attention [i]right now[/i], because that spot for your worker probably won't be around for your next turn.  The components and graphical presentation are top notch, and the gameplay is vicious.  A very flexible game, that can be satisfying with gamers of all stripes.  A little lengthier than many games of its type, but time flies while you're playing it.

RATING: N/A
It is the late 15th century and a new age is dawning. While searching for a new trade route to India, explorers have discovered a new land. The first reports tell of strange creatures, exotic people, and fabulous wealth. Captains and dventurers flock to these new lands in search of gold. They are quickly followed by colonists, soldiers, merchants, and missionaries all seeking wealth of one kind or another. Colonies begin to spring up, and soon competition among the great nations of Europe begins.
\13\ \13\
Take the role of one of Europe's colonial powers and stake your claim in the New World. As the leader of your nation, there are many paths that lead to victory: Discover and colonize new lands; acquire trade goods that will build your economy; develop new technologies and infrastructure in your home country; build your merchant fleet to dominate the trade routes; and build your army to defend what is rightfully yours!
\13\ \13\
The triumphant revel in riches and glory, while the vanquished become a footnote in the history books. It is an Age of Discovery... it is an Age of Empires!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I know that it's not even remotely original and that every single mechanism featured in the game has been repurposed from some other classic Euro. Hell, even the name was stolen, since Age of Empires III the computer game has absolutely nothing in common with the game found in this box. That being said, those stolen mechanisms work together like a finely tuned machine. Featuring aggressive player interaction and multiple paths to victory, AoE III does almost everything right.

The only misstep is the discovery system, which is blind, random and basically completely out of line with the rest of the game's zero-luck, deterministic nature. To me that's only a minor quibble though, as it's still possible to play conservatively and guarantee success. Of course if you stop and think about it, the game is about sailing off blindly into the unknown, so a little uncertainty in this particular facet of the game is probably justified.

It's one of my favorites, especially now that the expansion has added nation specific powers, which have added a ton of flavor and few new strategies to the mix.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The game is better than previous Drover designs, but ultimately very flawed. Money is the name of the game, and Discovery is the least worthwhile option. Too random. In a wargame, a bad die roll can hurt, but if it's one die roll amonst a ton of them, eventually things will even out. Here, you can plan your expedition for two turns (that's one fourth of the game!) and make sure you can beat the natives... and then you draw the poor man's discovery tile and don't get the reward you should have gotten for all your efforts. And that's unacceptable. Unless you admit that Discovery is NOT the way to go. Buildings are also unbalanced, which will hurt a lot when you've made sacrifices to go first next turn... and then the "weak" buidings pop out. Or when you've accepted to go last... and then the very best buildings come up. And it's not like, say, Puerto Rico, where every building can be just as valuable as the next one, depending on your situation: in Age of Empires, some buildings are ALWAYS better. And that's not good design. For instance, buildings that provide additional colonists/specialists are obviously overpowered. They give you more "actions" per turn, and they also help you get the last word (place the last colonist on the board). The more actions you get, the more actions you can fight to get, and the wheel keeps turning. Next to that, who cares about a building that gives you +1 soldier when you're attacked? It also makes the competition for the merchant ship very moot: few people will "risk it" when they see that one opponent still has a pile of colonists to play. The more I think about it, the more this feels like Risk: the rich get richer and it's very hard (impossible?) to tilt the scales. I would play it again, I suppose, but I would never ask for it myself.

RATING: 8
I enjoy this game.  It has great components, but to me what makes this a playable game, not buried in a lot of details, is the game board.  The design of the board is fantastic.  It lays everything out in logical fashion, so that it's very easy to see what your choices are, when everything takes place, etc.  Without this gameboard design, I'd have no interest in playing this because I wouldn't be able to keep track of everything.

I also like that there's many different valid strategies.  Lots of decisions to be made.

The main thing that holds me back from rating it higher is the game length, and of course that's very subjective.  For my tastes, for this time in my life, I'm less likely to suggest this than some other games which are just as fun, just as nice-looking, but play in a shorter amount of time.

UPDATE:  I think I wrote the above after 1, maybe 2 plays.  I've since played it more (not all of my plays are entered in the BGG database), and either the game plays faster with my familiarity with it, or it doesn't bother me now.  I've upped my rating, and maybe it should be a tad higher still.

RATING: N/A
Board game adaptation of the video game series. One of the first 4X worker placements for area control.  Has a distinct war game feel with cutthroat interaction. Distinct classic worker actions to colonize the New World provinces by displacing the indigenous populations. Interesting mechanic of refreshing pool of workers and timing when to buy the one time use of a special worker. Typical euro to build your economy, leverage the bonus building tiles to pay for expansion of colonies for victory points, sprinkle in some battles with your neighbors for control of a region. With plenty of opportunity to burn or be burned in negotiation.

RATING: 5.5
Played twice (with 4 and 5 players). I had high expectations for this game, which might explain why I am so disappointed. First of all, the components are not too good - the board is inconvenient (scoring track, small areas in the New World, no space for buildings and trade goods), the plastic bits would be ok, I guess, if trader/settler and captain/soldier were not so easily confused, and the box itself is flimsy. That wouldn't be too much of a concern if I actually enjoyed the game - but it seems to have too many weak points for that. The balance of the buildings is doubtful (early cash is worth a lot); the randomness of the buildings and trade goods draw means that playing for turn order is a gamble (no need to waste a turn to be first if there is nothing especially interesting or urgent to do); not all of the late buildings are going to appear in a game, meaning that some players might not have the ones really useful for their strategy (a possible 20% shift to their final score - much above what I can stand in a game that long); and the discovery phase... well, it is random, but I can live with the risk-taking and the possible loss of several units - but the random part which I really do not like, however, is that a guaranteed successful discovery, with several soldiers, could pay back anywhere between 5 and 20 doubloons, which definitely makes a difference.
On the other hand, I am not too bothered by the fact there are actually very few new mechanisms - it is just a pity that they haven't been integrated and balanced a bit better.

RATING: 7
Keith of Eagle Games brought the prototypes to a few of our club meetings for some playtesting. The game plays like Caylus meets El Grande with an old AH/SPI Conquistador theme. Game plays in 2 hours, but the 3 different ages keep the gameplay constantly evolving. Fan's of Martin Wallace should like this as the resources are tight, balanced choices are many, and the competition is fierce (with a small sprinkling of "attack the leader" via soldiers to keep the end score tight) - but if you hate random [i]post action[/i] exploration card results (and I've seen enough Railroad Tycoon threads to know that some people do) you might want to modify the rules some either playing them face up or discarding the extreme cards from play beforehand (fortunately the most extreme ones are in the early game giving time for counter strategies to trim off some game leaders score). 

Update: Have played the released version now and the minis and board art really help to evoke the theme. I did (but others in our groups didn't) have some problems telling the difference between merchant and colonist figures from the far end of the table. I might replace the colonists with a set of wooden cubes or glass beads in future plays. Or perhaps the answer is to do what is done with the missionaries and add a colonist along with any unit that is worth two units (e.g. when I add a captain to the merchant ship I also add a colonist from my unused supply with that captain and count each as one unit)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
{ P - 3 [4] 5 6 }

Worker placement done right. Paths to victory range from economic (trade goods, A3 buildings) to territorial (area control, exploration) with plenty of intermingling to balance a mixture of the two elements against outright specialization. (Example, some A3 buildings give points for new world presence, exploration leads to the acquisition of trade goods.) For added complexity workers can upgrade to specialists that perform more efficiently in two different functions. Buildings typically posit interesting dilemmas between points + single-use payoffs or annuity benefits. (The clunkers: Stables is pointless and should be removed. Cathedral is pretty linear because the only person who might want it is the guy who got the missionary building earlier. Migration - see below.) The randomness is a little irksome. It's not fun to be heavily naval and never see Privateers, collect sugar and never see Rum Distillery, or pick up an early gold and not see another until late A2. We play with two small changes:

[b]Migration on Bottom[/b]: This building at best moves you from second place to first in a colony. 4 VP. Worst building in A3. At least Militia is useful on a case-by-case basis. If this flops as part of the first five buildings (50% chance) of A3 and nobody buys it, during the last turn you only get four new buildings. Thus, one building is never available to buy. This kills whole strategies if that building just happens to be Power, Navy, Prosperity, Glory, or Mercantilism. Good players plan early-middle strategies around these buildings; no reason to let chance keep one on the sidelines. Easier to just put Migration on the bottom. Then if Militia is in the first set and nobody purchases it, Migration is the building that doesn't see play. Oh darn.

[b]Exploration Tiles are 3's and 4's[/b]: Not sure why there is so much variance on these. Nobody sends one or two guys on an expedition. Sending five is a pretty bad idea as well because the odds are substantially in your favor that four will succeed, and early game that extra guy is huge. But if you're the unlucky fellow who encounters what could be the only 5 on the board ... you probably just lost because failed expeditions tend to do that. Also, there's no good reason to have such a variance between how much each soldier can earn. Makes sense that so many people who play the game want to change just this aspect.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.75
Simply the best worker placement out there IMO. I love the fact that it has specialized roles yet you can clearly win the game without using any of them at all.

Great player interaction, scales incredibly well, easy enough to learn - but tons of strategic options and multiple paths to victory.  Every turn you have spots you "really want" to get which are taken up by other players.  This dynamic alone sets it apart from much of the competition due to the level of interactivity.

This game doesn't get its due because of the fact that it is ameritrash.  

.25 away from a 10 if it had better miniatures and smaller money pieces.  One of my top 3 games.

RATING: 8
Wow, what an awesome awesome game.  The way how the different elements interact and come together, the importance of when your turn is and the precedence of your selections, all intertwine to make it a very deep as well as strategic experience.  However, I do have one concern which deals with the replayability.  While there are many ways to victory, I do wonder if they are all created equal.  I am not totally convinced if there's any good way to beat the monastery, cathedral, migration and colonization tactic.  Maybe I was just lucky to have been able to purchase all those buildings which combined to win me the game by a big margin, but I am curious to find out if I can even achieve and surpass the points I was able to accumulate by using that method.

Update: Well, I finally got a chance to play AoE III again albeit in a tournament setting, and I have to say that a 5-player game is vastly different than one with 4.  Stupidly I tried to employ the same strategy that won my first game by forgetting that now there's one extra person competing for limited resources and spots on the board; not to mention I couldn't dominate the colonization scene with the Monastery-Cathedral-Population combo due to the fact that the first two buildings didn't even come up.  So there are definitely a bit more that meets the eyes, and the necessity to refine my strategy for future plays.

Update #2: It's been several months since I last played and rated AoE III, I have to honestly say that I haven't had the urge to want to get this on the table.  In all actuality I've avoided playing this on couple of occasions, therefore, I'm going to knock a fraction of point off, and if the feelings persist, it might go down even more.

RATING: 8.3
Rock solid game, here.  [person=1115]Glenn[/person] has come a long way since [thing=6707]Age of Mythology[/thing].  This game is laden with tough decisions and gambles.  While comparisons could be drawn to several respected games, [thing=22545]AoE3[/thing] gels into its own unique experience.  The miniatures are, of course, excess, but they're fun, and unlike [thing=6707]AoM[/thing], you needn't refer to cards for them the whole time.  This is a really good game, and it not only feels good, it also evokes the them quite nicely.  Minimizing your expeditions without risking failure...  Choosing which crucial action you need to nail first, so no one beats you to the punch, only to find that the other critical one is what people wanted...  Many ways to approach the game successfully.  This is a joy to play.  From 2 to 6 players, this game is fraught with tough decisions and delicate competition for resources.

Incidentally, using cubes on the score track works way better than using colonists!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Area control and worker placement game. Very good production with lots of miniatures, ships and plastic coins.

This is a solid game. There are quite some different strategy routes to follow, it's easy to explain and plays quickly. Combat is very basic and simple. It's certainly not a big conflict game, but just area control.   

Minor things that could be better are the small spaces to place your miniatures on in the Americas, they should have used the empty boardspace better. 

Thematically it lacks naval conflict. And all nations are the same : miniatures and what they do. This however doesn't detract from the gameplay. 

Another little thing is that it's unknown which buildings will appear for each game, so it's more difficult to set up a long term strategy. It however makes it also that you have to be more flexible.

Discoveries can be hit or miss even if you maximize your input of men. So this is actually my main concern. It's push your luck at great cost if you fail. 

Lastly it would have been cool if you could have moved your men around between colonies.   

It's not a topper nor has it something that blows you away, but it is very tightly designed and decent. 

A similar game is Dominant species, which is also longer to play and has more going on at the board. It has more options like wandering between spaces, unique factions, ice age mechanism... but AoE is shorter, quicker and tighter.

RATING: 6
A worker placement game that is quite rules-heavy. The theme works well and is nicely integrated. The components are well done (except for the score track around the board, which is way too small). The game isn't really very long, and even has some conflict, though said conflict involves no luck, and I like at least a dash of luck in my conflict. I suppose the main thing about this game that I don't like is that it doesn't stand out from the pack. This wouldn't be a problem if, every year, more and more excellent games were not released, but there are. This game is executed correctly, and if you're interested in the colonization theme, give it a go, but still - it doesn't exactly make itself known in a crowd of worker placement euros.
